[{'label': 'ARTICLE 1', 'text': '{\n  "sections": [\n    {\n      "heading": "Linking Soil Organic Carbon Stocks to Land-use Types in a Mediterranean Agroforestry Landscape",\n      "content": [\n        "V. H. DurÆn Zuazo 1 ∗ , C. R. Rodríguez Pleguezuelo , S. Cuadros Tavira , and J. R. 2 3 Francia Martínez 4"\n      ]\n    },\n    {\n      "heading": "ABSTRACT",\n      "content": [\n        "In agroforestry landscapes, land use, and the associated management practices exert strong impacts upon soil organic carbon stocks. Data on the soil organic carbon were collected for different land-use types within a small watershed, El Salado, located in Lanjarón (SE Spain). Eight land-use types namely: farmland planted in olive, almond, and cereals; forest with Pinus halepensis Mill. and Pinus sylvestris L. stands; shrubland; grassland; as well as abandoned farmland were taken into consideration. Of the land-use types investigated, forest, shrubland, as well as grassland exhibited the highest average soil organic C stocks (100-63 Mg ha -1 ) in contrast with the abandoned farmland (28 Mg ha -1 ), with farmland representing a go-between situation (51-49 Mg ha -1 ). The environmental factors precipitation, temperature, and elevation significantly influenced (P< 0.01) the soil organic C stock, with the contents tending to be higher in mountain soils with respective intermediate values of 600-800 mm, 10-15\'C, and 1,000-1,500 m asl. Thus, the present approach offers a comparison of C-sequestration patterns as related to the land-use types in a Mediterranean agroforestry landscape, where the main challenge is to integrate the forest trees and the crops within their harmonious interacting combinations.",\n        "Keywords : Land-use change, Lanjarón, Semiarid hillslopes, Soil-organic carbon, SE Spain ."\n      ]\n    },\n    {\n      "heading": "INTRODUCTION",\n      "content": [\n        "An estimation of carbon (C) stocks in soil and in vegetation is essential for a determination of the significance of C allocation in agroforestry landscapes, and as well in improving the understanding of their potential contribution to global C stocks (Calfapietra et al., 2010; Nair et al., 2010). It is also important to evaluate the impact of landuse/land-cover change on the terrestrial C balance, analysing its environmental consequences. Farmland abandonment and the conversion of forests and grasslands into farmland are known to deteriorate soil properties, and especially reduce soil organic matter (Lemenih et al., 2004; Ries and Hirt, 2008). In this sense, a study of the consequences of soil abandonment is very important, as time can play either for or against the trend of soil recovery (BlancoCanqui and Lal, 2008). In general, when agricultural land is no longer used for cultivation, but allowed to be either reverted to natural vegetation or replanted into perennial vegetation, Soil Organic Carbon (SOC) can",\n        "\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_",\n        "1 Institute of Agricultural and Fisheries Research and Training (IFAPA) Centro \'Las Torres-Tomejil\', Carretera Sevilla-Cazalla km 12,2. 41200, AlcalÆ del Río, Sevilla, Spain.",\n        "∗ Corresponding author; email: victorh.duran@juntadeandalucia.es",\n        "2 Earth and Life Institute - Environmental Sciences (ELI-e), UniversitØ Catholique de Louvain. Croix du Sud 2, L7.05.02 B-1348 Louvain-la-Neuve, Belgium.",\n        "3 University of Cordoba, Campus de Rabanales Crta. Nacional IV A km 396, 14071 Córdoba, Spain.",\n        "Institute of Agricultural and Fisheries Research and Training (IFAPA) Centro \'Camino de Purchil\'.",\n        "4 Apdo. 2027, 18080 Granada, Spain.",\n        "<!-- image -->",\n        "\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_",\n        "then accumulate through processes that are contrary to the effects responsible for SOC losses inflicted from when the land was converted from its perennial vegetation (Post and Kwon, 2000). However, in this particular Mediterranean environment, those cultivated fields (usually vineyards and olive trees) are prone to enhance soil degradation processes and cause to be impaired if abandoned at this stage (Dunjo et al., 2003).",\n        "The relationship between land-use systems and climate change is fundamental, as these systems sequester atmospheric CO2 and store the C in soil and as well in plants. Consequently, land-cover dynamics, particularly deforestation, forest fire, as well as abandoned farmlands, have become critical concerns, as the implications for systems involving human livelihoods are immense (Bowen et al., 2007; DurÆn et al., 2013). In this context, sustainable utilization and conservation of natural resources in Mediterranean agroforestry landscapes is one of the fundamental components of sustainable rural development (Cacho, 2001; DurÆn et al., 2011).",\n        "Also, plant-soil feedback loops influence both plant-community and soil conservation, making researchers increasingly aware of how the aboveground and belowground relationships govern terrestrial-ecosystem processes. According to Bezemer et al. (2006), plant-soil feedback mechanisms depend on plant species, functional groups, and sitespecific differences in biotic and as well in abiotic chemical characteristics of the soil. On the other hand, climatic factors, especially temperature and precipitation, are the prime determinants of SOC contents (Liu et al., 2011).",\n        "Mediterranean agroforestry systems are considered as possessing a higher potential to sequester atmospheric C because of their recognised greater ability to gather and utilize such growth resources as light, nutrients, and water in comparison with single-species crops or grassland systems. As stated by Bolin and Sukumar (2000), land-use change is associated with changes in plant cover and in C stock. Moreover, Senthil et al. (2006) reported that the ability to sequester C in the soil is modulated by topography, management practices, initial SOC, soil properties, and as well by parent materials. Therefore, the soil can maintain a potential C-stock equilibrium between C inputs vs. losses. However, this balance can be upset by land-use change until a new equilibrium is established within the transformed ecosystem. In this context, many recent studies have demonstrated the strong impact of land-use types on SOC stocks and their fractionation in hilly and semiarid regions (Mokhtari et al., 2012; Ayoubi et al., 2012).",\n        "The present work, through an analysis of soil C stocks for the dominant land-cover types in a given land use, contributes knowledge on potential C sequestration, thereby improving the estimation not only of the organic C stock in a Mediterranean agroforestry landscape but also of the potential changes in SOC content, due to different scenarios involving various land-use types, using the example of the Watershed El Salado, Granada (SE Spain)."\n      ]\n    },\n    {\n      "heading": "MATERIALS AND METHODS",\n      "content": []\n    },\n    {\n      "heading": "The Study Area",\n      "content": [\n        "The study area, a watershed of 669.7 ha named El Salado, situated in the Sierra Nevada Mountains near Lanjarón (province of Granada, SE Spain; Figure 1-A), ranges from 2,374 m to 670 m asl, with an average slope exceeding 20%. The features of the watershed are common of the in Mediterranean mountain zones. Some of the main representative Land-Use Types (LUT) are presented in Figure 1-B. Table 1 provides a brief description of the vegetation species in each unit of land use.",\n        "The dominant soil parent material is colluvium and residuum derived from mica schist, while the slopes being composed predominantly of phyllites and mica-schist, with weathered regolith covers of only a few cm depths. These phyllites may have been overlain in places by limestones that have rafted downslope on top of the phyllites. In general, the soils are of loamy, sandy-loam, and silt-loam textures, as classified according to the FAO-WRB (1998) (Figure 1-C).",\n        "Table 1. Specifically, sampling plots were defined and appointed for each land use namely for olive, almond, Pinus halepensis Mill., and Pinus sylvestris L. stands. Other plots were laid out for cereals, shrubland, grassland, and as well for abandoned farmlands; these later plots having been left to grow without any human disturbance since 2007, when the farmland was practically abandoned (Table 1).",\n        "The soil was sampled (0-25 and 25-50 cm depths) within each selected land-cover type to represent the land use type investigated. For shrubland, grassland, and abandoned farmlands which were the most common LUTs and for those expected to show high variations C, a larger number of subplots (n= 45) was made use of. As for farmland and forests, which were expected to show lower variations in of C level, fewer subplots (n= 20) were take into account.",\n        "Bulk Density (BD), and soil texture were determined using standard methods (MAPA, 1994). The quantity of SOC stored in the soil was adjusted through soil mass. An estimation of organic C stock at 0.5 m soil depth was made using the following equation:",\n        "<!-- formula-not-decoded -->",\n        "Where, A is the area (ha); BD the Soil Bulk Density (t m -3 ) at 0.5 m depth; f C the fraction of C , and while H standing for the soil depth (m).",\n        "For mapping of the SOC in the watershed, Kriging Interpolation was made use of (Kriging Interpolator 3.2 3D, extension with Arcview 3.2 Spatial Analyst, Nieuwland Automatisering, Wageningen, Netherlands) to interpolate the surface for the Digital Elevation Model (DEM) of 5-m resolution. Also, regression Kriging was chosen to interpolate the data because it is commonly used as an unbiased estimator to minimize error variance.",\n        "For an analysis of the effect of environmental factors on organic C stocks, and for an overall estimate of the impact at the watershed level, the elevation, rainfall, and temperature were divided into three categories (< 1,000, 1,000-1,500, and > 1,500 m asl; < 600, 600-800, and > 800 mm; and <10, 10-15 and 15°C, respectively).",\n        "Soil samples were air-dried before being passed through 2-mm sieves, with crop debris, root material, and stones being removed. The Soil Organic Carbon (SOC),",\n        "Analysis of variance was performed to ascertain whether the land uses differed in C stocks. Differences between individual means were assessed using the Least Significant Difference (LSD) test at P < 0.05 in Statgraphics v. 5.1. Finally, the data were",\n        "Table 2. Conversely, there was a tendency of increase in SOC content with soil depth in olive, almond, and abandoned farmland. In particular, the SOC content was high in P. halepensis Mill. at 0-25 cm of soil depth (more than 50%) in comparison with the other LUTs, especially for almond, olive, and abandoned farmlands. Since plant covers have access to the organic-matter input of C to the soil, the C content is",\n        "<!-- image -->",\n        "expected to be greater at the soil surface than in deeper layers. In addition, according to Johnson and Curtis (2001), the greater soil C and N stock in the coniferous forests might have resulted from the litterfall, more plentiful under P. halepensis Mill. and P. sylvestris L. This result agrees with a study by Jeddi and Chaieb (2010), who concluded that forests with P. halepensis Mill. improved the soil by increasing organic C, and well by facilitating the colonization and development of understory vegetation. Also, in shrublands dominated by diverse plants, the organic C was presumably high due to the incorporation of decomposing plant litter (Rodríguez et al., 2009). Similar results have been reported by Fu et al. (2004), who found the level of organic C content to be lower in farmland than in land rich with native vegetation. In addition, according to Melero et al. (2011) the tillage practices used may accelerate the decomposition rate of organic matter, as in monitored farmland plots. According to the results of the observational studies, grassland could be considered an intermediate step between farmland and shrubland, as shown in Figure 2, where plant diversity is shown to increase the short-term",\n        "Table 3. The results clearly demonstrate that soils beneath the vegetation with P. halepensis Mill., P. sylvestris L., and shrubland added fertility where organic C densities were greater (10.03, 8.31, and 7.20 kg m -2 , respectively) than the remaining LUTs. In this context, Janssens et al. (1999), studying a P. sylvestris stand, reported higher values for C stored in soils (11.5 kg m -2 ) while PØrez et al. (2011), examining the soil C stocks of native and afforested arid Mediterranean shrubland, found lower values (3.2 and 1.9 kg m -2 , respectively) than those in the present experiment. In addition, the estimated soil C stock in different LUTs are close to those reported by Miralles et al. (2009) in mountain agroecosystems of SE Spain: reforested pine forest < 60 years old ( P. halepensis ) of 72.5 Mg ha -1 , thickets (shrubland) 64.7 Mg ha -1 , and crops (almond, olive, and cereals) 51.8 Mg ha -1 .",\n        "<!-- image -->",\n        "vegetation patches, especially in abandoned farmland areas, where slow and progressive plant succession took place, as depicted in Figure 2.",\n        "Grassland significantly (P< 0.05) increased organic C stocks as compared with the abandoned farmland conditions, improving soil properties. These changes in soil-surface conditions are presumably related to a contribution of organic C by the leaves and roots of annuals and as well to the perennial species inhabiting the",\n        "A comparison of the mean values of the different farmlands (olive, almond, and cereals) shows similarities in the SOC stock (Table 4). Analogous stocks were pointed out by Murillo (2001) for Mediterranean Spain: 5.1 to 5.8 kg C m -2 for arable lands, and 3.9 kg C m -2 for olive orchards. In addition, similar stocks for French and Italian environments of 4.9 and 5.2 kg C m -2 for farmlands were recorded by Martin et al. (2011) and Chiti et al. (2012), respectively. Consequently, agricultural soils, and especially eroded ones, usually contain lower organic C stocks than their potential capacity, as corroborated by olive, almond, and cereal plots. Thus, the natural and progressive recolonization of abandoned land by spontaneous native species, appropriate soil-management systems in farmland (i.e. non-tillage, reduced tillage, plant strips, etc.), and management of forestland as well as scrubland can augment organic C stocks through C sequestration (Chivenge et al., 2007; DurÆn et al., 2011) (Figure 2). In Figure 3, the map reveals that the land-use type affected the spatial distribution of SOC within the watershed. Shrubland, at all elevation intervals, contained almost similar mean SOC contents. P. halepensis Mill. and P. sylvestris L. stands distributed at elevations of 1,000-1,500 m and > 1,500 m, respectively, showed higher SOC contents than did other LUTs. The effect of elevation on the main climate parameters differed with a positive vs. negative association of elevation vs. precipitation (r= 0.98), and elevation vs. temperature (r= -0.96), respectively. Since temperatures tend to decline with rises in elevation Leifeld et al. (2005), this could have reduced the SOC turnover rates in the study area. In this context, Figure 4A shows SOC content in relation to elevation, registering relatively higher values",\n        "On the other hand, Figure 4-B also displays the influence of precipitation on SOC density under LUTs, the types in the present study belonging to the intermediate level of 600-800 mm. Under farmland, precipitation did not strongly influence SOC. However, such agricultural activities in the watershed, as tillage and crop-waste management, accentuated the decline in SOC accumulation. Contrary to the forest, particularly of Pinus stands, SOC density was significantly higher in zones with precipitation of 600-800 mm as compared with rainfall of > 800 mm. The high SOC content in shrubland, grassland, and forest under either relatively high or low precipitations could be attributed to slow decomposition of litter. In addition, different responses of LUTs to precipitation could be ascribed to the nature of plant species and assorted complex interactions among them and as well to climate circumstances. In this sense, Figure 4C indicates the influence of temperature on SOC under the LUTs monitored. When a LUT was not considered, SOC density was relatively lower in areas where temperature was > 15\'C than in intermediate (5-15\'C) and cooler areas (< 10\'C). All the LUTs studied had intermediate precipitation (600-800 mm) in the areas with temperatures between 10 and 15\'C. A combination of intermediate temperatures and wetter conditions may lead to better biomass production and potentially higher SOC accumulation. Comparatively,",\n        "<!-- image -->",\n        "Figure 4. Mean soil organic carbon content at 0-50 cm of soil depth at different elevations (A), precipitation (B), and temperature (C) for different land-use types of the El Salado watershed in Lanjarón, Granada (SE, Spain). CER, cereal; GRL, grassland; OLI, olive; ALM, almond; PHA, Pinus halepensis ; PSY, Pinus sylvestris ; SHR, shrub; AFL, Abandoned FarmLand. Vertical bars represent standard deviations.",\n        "<!-- image -->",\n        "<!-- image -->",\n        "\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_\\\\_",\n        "higher content SOC values in fields with temperatures lower than 10\'C may be ascribed to the slower microbial and chemical breakdown of organic residues.",\n        "The influence of temperature on SOC also diverged among the LUTs, being higher under Pinus stands and shrubland in all the temperature categories. This trend was consistent with the overall effect of rainfall and temperature on SOC stocks, as mentioned above, suggesting that the factor governing either the presence or absence of forests in these areas was elevation.",\n        "However, the impact of environmental factors is complex because of the association between elevation and climatic parameters, where at intermediate elevations the wetter and warmer conditions could encourage biomass production, boosting the organic carbon inputs to the soil. Therefore, these findings suggest that land uses under intermediate climatic and elevation conditions could be considered as potential sinks for the sequestration of the atmospheric C.",\n        "The results of the correlation analysis revealed that SOC was in significant relationships (P< 0.01) with elevation (r= 0.59), temperature (r= -0.60), and precipitation (r= 0.61). In total, the relationship of SOC vs. elevation was also investigated, and positive correlations found, in agreement with Mendoza et al. (2003). Furthermore, the increasing trend of SOC with rainfall, in the study area, could be attributed to the joint interactions with temperature and the level of net primary production in controlling the overall soil C stocks along such an elevational gradient. Finally, for the study area, a typical decline in vegetation was noted across elevational strata and within locations, grassland being the dominant LUT."\n      ]\n    },\n    {\n      "heading": "CONCLUSIONS",\n      "content": [\n        "Land-use change from forest to abandoned farmland significantly reduced the organic C accumulation in the soil. The results of the present study provide insight into the potential benefits of forest and shrubland to act as C sinks, indicating that, when agricultural practices are stopped, the abandoned farmlands are led to a shift in vegetation composition in the sense that grass species are replaced by shrubdominated communities. Such shifts boost the capability of the atmospheric C to be fixed in these types of ecosystems. Consequently, the potential of forestry and shrubland in C sequestration should be considered for appropriate management in order to maximize CO2 sequestering as well as to balance CO emissions. In particular, 2 the present results suggest that Pinus stands are effective in sequestering the organic C in soils, explaining the predominance of climate over vegetation in influencing the organic C stock at higher elevations, and of vegetation type as well as physiographic situation at lower elevations within the watershed. In addition, land uses under intermediate weather and elevation conditions could be regarded as potential sinks for the sequestration of the atmospheric C.",\n        "The positive relationship found between elevation and SOC stocks in the present study implies that a proportion of the shift in the level of SOC is explained by the climate, or is due to the influence of predominant vegetation and plant species related to an elevational gradient. Thus, a knowledge of soil C stocks and the effects of environmental factors on them are critical, both from the perspective of C budgets and the corresponding Mediterranean agroforestry management."\n      ]\n    },\n    {\n      "heading": "ACKNOWLEDGEMENTS",\n      "content": [\n        "Part of this study that leads to this publication was sponsored by the research project \'Conservation agriculture techniques in rainfed-tree crops and Mediterranean climate: implications for sustainable productivity, erosion control, and improvement of soil quality and",\n        "biodiversity\' (RTA2011-00007-00-00) granted by INIA, Spain and cofinanced by FEDER funds (European Union)."\n      ]\n    },\n    {\n      "heading": "REFERENCES",\n      "content": [\n        "- 1. Tropics and Subtropics (380a)",\n        "Oliver John Zemek M.Sc. Thesis",\n        "BIOMASS AND CARBON STOCKS INVENTORY OF PERENNIAL VEGETATION IN THE CHIENG KHOI WATERSHED, NW VIET NAM"\n      ]\n    },\n    {\n      "heading": "Supervisors",\n      "content": [\n        "Prof. Dr. Georg Cadisch",\n        "(Department for Agroecology and Plant Production in the Tropics and Subtropics) Prof. Dr. Sauerborn (Department for Agroecology and Plant Production in the Tropics and Subtropics) Dr. Carsten Marohn (Department for Agroecology and Plant Production in the Tropics and Subtropics) Dr. Thomas Hilger (Department for Agroecology and Plant Production in the Tropics and Subtropics)"\n      ]\n    },\n    {\n      "heading": "Abstract",\n      "content": [\n        "With climate change being unequivocal, reducing CO2 in our atmosphere has become a primary goal of international efforts. Perennial terrestrial vegetation can be an important sink of atmospheric carbon. Clean development mechanism may provide incentives to preserve or even increase areas under perennial vegetation. Implementation of such measures requires reliable estimates of standing biomass under perennial vegetation, which are more difficult to obtain than for annual mono-cropping systems.",\n        "The commune of Chieng Khoi has, like most of the northern mountainous region of Viet Nam, witnessed drastic changes in land use during the last decades. Studies are lacking that provide primary data on perennial biomass and related carbon stocks, and at the same time assess the local potential for and benefits from mitigating climate change. This study intends to bridge this gap by quantifying the biomass and C-stocks stored in perennial vegetation of the Chieng Khoi commune. Using a nested sampling plot design, this study surveyed 10 perennial land-use systems. Above-ground biomass parameters of trees were measured non-destructively and parameters of mid- and under-storey vegetation and coarse litter were sampled destructively. Allometric equations for Melastoma sanguineum Sims, Chromolaena odorata (L.) and local bamboo species were developed. To compare the different land-use types the obtained data was time-averaged. At stand ages of 12 and 20 years, aboveground carbon stocks of timber and fruit tree plantations differed significantly. Moreover, our study\'s results showed a reduction of 61% in total communal carbon stocks during the last 50 years, even though there was only a decrease in forested area of 36%, possibly due to differing temporal and overall carbon storing capacities of natural forest vegetation and tree based plantation systems. Overall, our study provides input data that help to improve modeling approaches such as the Land Use Change Impact Assessment tool (LUCIA). Such models estimate biomass and carbon stocks of the terrestrial vegetation, and contribute to identifying land use types as well as management options that may lead to ecological and economic benefits for local farmers and international stakeholders, seeking possibilities to reduce CO2 emissions.",\n        "Key Words: NW Viet Nam, biomass, carbon stocks, land -use change, allometric equation, perennial vegetation, clean development mechanism, time-averaged"\n      ]\n    },\n    {\n      "heading": "Author\'s declaration",\n      "content": [\n        "I, Zemek, Oliver John (matriculation number: 439749, University of Hohenheim),",\n        "hereby affirm that I have written this Master Thesis titled: \'BIOMASS AND CARBON STOCKS INVENTORY OF PERENNIAL VEGETATION IN THE CHIENG KHOI WATERSHED, NW VIET NAM\', independently and by myself.",\n        "All authors mentioned in this work have been cited and no work has been included in the Thesis without the authors being listed. "Background",\n      "content": [\n        "This study was conducted within the framework of the C 4 subproject of the Special Research Program SFB 564-The Uplands Program, and the Trees in Multi-Use Landscapes in Southeast Asia (TUL-SEA) project of the World Agroforestry Centre, Bogor, Indonesia (in collaboration with the country office ICRAF Viet Nam).",\n        "In this context two simulation models are presently used and evaluated in Thailand and Viet Nam. One model is the Rapid Carbon Stock Appraisal model (RaCSA) developed within the TUL-SEA project. The RaCSA model integrates biomass and C-stock inventories as one of 6 steps, to simulate and generate win-win policies for a land-use change that sustainably benefits local people and at the same time helps to mitigate climate change (v. Noordwijk 2009).",\n        "The second model is the Land Use Change Impact Assessment model (LUCIA). It has been developed in the framework of the Uplands Program (SFB 564) for the mountainous regions of northern Thailand and Viet Nam. LUCIA assesses impacts of land use change and management on soil and hydrological functions, plant productivity and environmental services such as carbon sequestration potentials (SFB 564 2009).",\n        "While the TUL-SEA project specifically assesses strategies and management options associated with integrating trees as part of a participatory development and resource assessment, the overall objective of the SFB 564 is to develop and integrate sustainable land use with sustainable rural development. Hereby the different subprojects of the SFB 564 aim at one common goal, i.e. in a sustainable way to reduce rural poverty and food insecurity in mountainous regions of Southeast Asia (SFB 564 2009, TUL-SEA 2009)."\n      ]\n    },\n    {\n      "heading": "1 Introduction",\n      "content": [\n        "One of the research areas within the SFB 564 is the Chieng Khoi commune located in Son La province, northwest of Viet Nam. Like many areas in Southeast Asia, the Chieng Khoi watershed has undergone drastic changes in land-use due to agricultural intensification and population increase over the last decades. These land use changes have often led to erosion and a loss of soil fertility, in particular of upland soils (Wezel et al. 2002, Trieu 2004).",\n        "Moreover, as various studies on land-use change through deforestation and degradation of natural forests show, diminishing overall carbon storage capacities in vegetation aboveand belowground and in soils may have resulted thereof (Brown and Lugo 1984, Brown 1997, FAO 2004, Nikolic et al. 2008).",\n        "The latter causal connection has been also described by Houghton and Hackler (1999). In their study, two types of land-use change are distinguished: conversion of natural forest to cultivated crop land and the reverse, i.e. reforestation, and secondly, activities that don\'t change the forest area (ha) but the carbon density (Mg/ha), i.e. shifting cultivation, logging, fires, grazing of woodland and (fuel)-wood harvest, which depending on management can lead to a loss or accumulation of carbon.",\n        "However, Houghton and Hackler (1999) state that the major contributor of carbon to atmosphere is the conversion of natural forest into cultivated crop land, as 20-50 times more C is stored in forest than in agricultural land, resulting in a release of 100-200 MgC/ha, without counting the loss from C stored in the soil, which can be in a few years time as much as 25% of the initially stored carbon in the top 100cm of soil (Davidson and Ackermann, 1993 cited in Houghton and Hackler 1999).",\n        "In other words, land-use change, as practiced over the last decades, is not only leading to diminished overall carbon storage capacities in above- and belowground vegetation, evermore as local carbon flows are altered through deforestation and degradation of natural vegetation, an increased release of carbon to the atmosphere, through enhanced soil respiration, is expected (Houghton and Hackler 1999, FAO 2004).",\n        "Realizing the latter and consequences thereof, whilst considering that a high percentage of Vietnamese people still depend on non-timber forest products, the Vietnamese government and non governmental organizations (NGOs) launched different initiatives, such as national reform programs 327, 661 and 135 and the Social Forestry Development Project",\n        "(SFDP 1993-2004) of the Deutsche Gesellschaft für Technische Zusammenarbeit (GTZ), respectively, to foster reforestation, protection of natural forests and afforestation (GTZ 2004, Trieu 2004).",\n        "In this context, over the last two decades, plantations of new and improved timber and fruit tree cultivars such as Pinus ssp. , Tectona grandis , Chukrasia tabularis , bamboo, Dimocarpus longan , Mangifera indica , Litchi sinensis , Diospyros kaki and Cinnamonum sp. were introduced (GTZ 2004, Trieu 2004). Furthermore forest logging in Viet Nam per year was limited by law from 1081 * 10³ m³ in 1990 to 250 * 10³ m³ in 2003 (Trieu 2004).",\n        "However, in Chieng Khoi commune these efforts were mainly done in order to reduce erosion on the highly vulnerable sloping upland fields and thereby decrease siltation of rice paddies and irrigation water catchments in the lowlands and to partially satisfy the increased demand for timber products (Trieu 2004, Meyfroidt and Lambin 2008).",\n        "Today with climate change receiving more attention, the question arises whether the efforts were reasonable with regard to carbon sequestration, or if there is a need for adapting strategies in order to face and solve two goals simultaneously, i.e. combating climate change and reducing erosion? Several studies have been and are still being conducted in the Chieng Khoi commune with regard to erosion control. However, studies providing primary data concerning biomass and carbon stocks, which also assess the local potential for and benefits from mitigating climate change, respectively, are lacking.",\n        "This study tries to bridge this gap by quantifying the biomass and C-stocks stored in perennial vegetation of the Chieng Khoi commune."\n      ]\n    },\n    {\n      "heading": "2 Hypothesis",\n      "content": [\n        "Changes in land use associated with agricultural intensification often lead to a reduced sequestration potential for CO2. Land use change within Chieng Khoi commune has been drastic over the last decades. Simulation models can be used to dynamically estimate carbon stocks and flows under different land use scenarios, which can help policy makers to evaluate, decide on and implement Clean Development Mechanisms (CDM) or Reducing Emissions from Deforestation and Forest Degradation in Developing Countries (REDD) projects. To run such models, primary data on biomass and especially C stocks of perennial vegetation are needed for parameterization, calibration and validation. The main objective of this study was to provide field data on above-ground biomass (AGB) of perennial vegetation, including different stages of natural succession as well as plantations of fruit and timber species. In this context, the following hypotheses were tested:",\n        "- · Regarding carbon densities (on a Mg per ha basis), the following ranking of Cstocks between land uses was assumed: Grassland < bush fallow < fruit tree plantations < timber tree plantations < secondary forest < bamboo forest < protected primary forest.",\n        "- · Regarding carbon densities (Mg/ha), fruit and timber tree plantations do not significantly differ inter-specifically 12 years after planting.",\n        "- · Using time-averaged C-stock data, fruit and timber tree plantations differ in carbon densities (Mg/ha) from extensively managed fallow land (surveyed forests) after a time period of 20 years.",\n        "- · Due to decreasing forest cover during the last decades in Chieng Khoi it has been argued that carbon stocks have been decreased drastically. However, we assume that given the effect of reforestation, this decrease is small."\n      ]\n    },\n    {\n      "heading": "3 Objectives",\n      "content": [\n        "The Chieng Khoi commune represents the economically less developed mountainous regions of Southeast Asia.",\n        "In search for reducing CO2 emissions from land-use change, carbon flows have to be analyzed in order to make decisions on reversing unsustainable land-use changes. Reforestation, protection and regenerating natural vegetation can be an option for sustainable development and improved livelihoods for local farmers.",\n        "Yet, as a first step the potential and status quo of C-stocks in the Chieng Khoi commune has to be evaluated, as knowledge about biomass and carbon stocks is of importance to calibrate and parameterize simulation models such as LUCIA.",\n        "Due to the lack of primary data, to test the aforementioned hypotheses the main objective of this study was to conduct a biomass and C-stock inventory of perennial vegetation in the Chieng Khoi commune.",\n        "Therefore specific objectives of this study were to:",\n        "- · Identify the most relevant perennial land use types in Chieng Khoi",\n        "- · Describe these land-use types in terms of floristic composition and structure",\n        "- · Measure input parameters required to formulate allometric equations for overand mid-storey species",\n        "- · Compare and select allometric equations for over-storey species from literature",\n        "- · Destructively measure and define own allometric equations for mid-storey vegetation",\n        "- · Destructively determine biomass of undergrowth vegetation",\n        "- · Add up the different stocks weighted by the share of the respective canopy storey and thus derive carbon stocks per land use class",\n        "- · Determine the respective area of each land use class under consideration using existing GIS data and calculate total aboveground carbon stocks",\n        "- · Generate input data for the model parameterization of LUCIA for optimizing existing parameterization equations for different types of natural vegetation."\n      ]\n    },\n    {\n      "heading": "4 Literature review",\n      "content": [\n        "The Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change\'s (IPCC) findings on global warming are more certain and alarming than ever. It has been shown unequivocally that most of the observed increase in global average temperatures is \'very likely\' due to the observed steady increase of CO2 in our atmosphere, i.e. from 280 parts per million (ppm) in 1850 up to 387 ppm in 2009 (IPCC 2007, NOAA 2009).",\n        "To avoid and combat this dangerous climate change 187 nations have so far ratified the Kyoto Protocol. In this year\'s Conference of Parties (COP 15) summit in Copenhagen these nations will have to decide upon stringent ways on how to combat climate change in a post Kyoto era, in order to achieve a stabilization in carbon dioxide concentrations below 450ppm till 2050, and beyond. However the ways, the response of mankind has to be reducing its GHG emissions, in particular carbon dioxide if the effects of global warming should be kept at an acceptable level (IPCC 2007, UN REDD 2009a).",\n        "A prerequisite for reducing CO2 emissions will be to achieve a better understanding of the global carbon cycle. Secondly, to clearly identify and quantify major reservoirs of carbon as well as sinks and sources of carbon emissions. Lastly options of CO2 reduction have to be developed, implemented, evaluated and adapted.",\n        "So far, studies show the complexity of the global carbon cycle, as it includes an interwoven exchange of carbon among the different reservoirs of carbon, i.e. biosphere, lithosphere, hydrosphere and atmosphere of the earth. All four spheres differ in their temporal and overall storage capacity and therefore can be sources and sinks of carbon depending upon the carbon exchange-balance amongst each other, also called the carbon budgets (Sedjo 1993).",\n        "About four times the amount of carbon present in our atmosphere is stored in terrestrial ecosystems, whereof about one third is stored above ground and two thirds are stored belowground (Watson et al. 1998, IPCC 2007, Nair et al. 2009). In particular the long-term storage capacity of carbon in tree above- and BGB in various kinds of natural forests and commercial plantations seem to be a promising option for carbon sequestration (UN REDD 2009a).",\n        "However, as discussed in a report by Carrere and González (2000), carbon storage of natural primary forests and plantations differs in terms of temporal and overall storing",\n        "capacity. In an example they state that old primary forests can store large amounts of carbon, in particular belowground for 1000 years or more. When forests are cleared this long-term carbon is released. This agrees with findings by Nikolic et al. (2008) in dampening the euphoria about tree plantations, stating that these mono-cultural systems on the one hand can not sequester and store the same amount of carbon stored in natural vegetation due to reduced biodiversity and on the other because of early maximum CO2 capture capacities.",\n        "Yet, annually 11.6 million ha of natural forest are lost in the tropics due to deforestation, degradation and desertification, especially due to an increased pressure on forests from demographic, economic and social changes (Archard et al. 2002, Grace 2004, FAO 2006). Overall land-use change contributes to nearly 20% of all green house gas (GHG) emissions (UN REDD 2009a).",\n        "When looking at Southeast Asia, Houghton and Hackler (1999) showed that total emissions of carbon to the atmosphere due to land-use change and forestry changes from 1850-1995 were (however, at an uncertainty of 50%) 43.5 petagram (Pg) of CO2, whereof 33.5 PgCO2 originated from clearing of forests into permanent crop lands and 11.5 PgCO2 from shifting cultivation, logging and fuel-wood extraction.",\n        "However, since 1980 a steady decline of carbon emissions from land-use change in Southeast-Asia was registered by Houghton and Hackler (1999). In particular plantations which were insignificant before 1960, but in 1990 already covered 22.6 x 10 ha of 6 Southeast Asia\'s land area had an average annual net uptake of 0.1 PgCO2 per year. Moreover, according to the IPCC (2003, cited in Nair et al. 2009) worldwide a net uptake of 1.1 to 1.6 PgCO2 per year, of which two thirds would be in the tropics, could be achieved by reforestation and afforestation activities in the time period 1995-2050.",\n        "In response to this understanding, the United nations (UN) developed two instruments focusing on this assumed sink-source potential of the terrestrial vegetation, i.e. the United Nations Collaborative Program on Reducing Emissions from Deforestation and Forest Degradation in Developing Countries (REDD) and the Clean Development Mechanism, the so called CDM.",\n        "While the UN-REDD Program aims at providing counseling and benefiting developing countries financially for the protection, reforestation and sustainable management of their",\n        "forest resources, CDM projects aim at encouraging the industrial countries to fund projects in developing countries, which reduce or even sequester GHG emissions. For this funding the initiating party receives so called certified emission reduction units (CERs, equal to 1 metric ton of CO2 equivalent), which in return can be traded on carbon markets to ensure an economically efficient compliance on agreed reduction targets of the Kyoto protocol (UNFCCC 2009a).",\n        "Since 1991, The Socialist Republic of Viet Nam is one of the designated pilot tropical nonAnnex I countries participating in the UN-REDD Program (GFRA 2005, UN REDD 2009b).",\n        "So far Viet Nam has been granted financial support of US$ 4.38 Mio for meeting set targets and regulations, e.g. a periodical (5-year intervals for Viet Nam) report according to the United Nations Framework Convention on Climate Change\'s (UNFCCC) Good Practice Guidelines (GPG) upon its land use change and carbon stocks (UN REDD 2009b). Moreover, 11 CDM projects of worldwide 1895 projects have been registered in Viet Nam till 2009 (UNFCCC 2009b).",\n        "Recent statistical numbers up to 2005 from the UN-REDD (UN REDD 2009c) show, that since participating in the REDD program, forest cover (in total of 12.9 million ha, i.e. 40% of the total land area) in Viet Nam increased against the global trend by 3.6 million hectares, or on average 0.24 million hectares annually (2% per year). Looking at carbon stocks based on biomass estimates, Viet Nam\'s forests sequester 0.947 Pg, 0.227 Pg, 0.156 Pg and 0.623 Pg of carbon above ground, below ground, in dead wood and litter and in the soil, respectively.",\n        "Yet, considering the recent REDD national capacity report (Herold 2009) this positive trend of available figures has to be handled with caution, as it is stated that there is \'actually, a continuous loss of natural forests […] for Viet Nam\' and that \'the increase [in forest area] is mainly attributed to expanding plantation forests, regenerating grass/shrub land and above all to a statistical effect.\'",\n        "Therefore to achieve a higher reporting status the report demands for Viet Nam to improve its forest area change monitoring system, i.e. at first carbon measurements, which still provide inconsistent data due to an insufficient monitoring program to cover forest",\n        "degradation. In addition, there is a need for enhanced nationwide systematic and standardized methods for the quantification of above- and BGB and soil carbon.",\n        "Secondly, infrastructure, mainly on provincial and local level, i.e. hardware, software and a better internet connectivity is needed to conduct, analyze and process forest monitoring and inventory data, respectively. Thirdly, human resources are needed, i.e. skilled personal capable to assess, collect, process, analyze and report the information resulting from the measurement and monitoring of biomass and carbon stocks as well as forest degradation.",\n        "Lastly it is expressed that the development of clearly identified \'national carbon stock key categories, country specific field sampling designs and stratification methods, which include parameters such as allometric data for biomass conversion and expansion, carbon fraction values considering country-specific stratification and disturbance matrices that track transfers between carbon pools\' is needed (Herold 2009).",\n        "So far different tools for observation have been developed, such as global vegetation maps, satellite based infrared monitoring, e.g. the Normalized Differenced Vegetation Index (NDVI) and computer-based modeling approaches. However, up to date these tools are not immune to problems, be it actuality or obstruction by weather conditions, e.g. clouds (mean annual cloud coverage of Viet Nam 77%, Herold 2009). Therefore regional specific, ground proofed methods to measure and monitor biomass and carbon stocks are still indispensable.",\n        "Summarizing studies from Cairns et al. (1997), Clark and Clark (2000), Brown (1997, 2002), Hairiah et al. (2001) Snowdon et al. (2002), Nascimento and Laurance (2002), Ponce-Hernandez (2004) and Chave et al. (2004) commonly the following steps have to be considered in order to collect primary data to set up a biomass and carbon stock inventory.",\n        "First the research area has to determined and specified according to climatic conditions, vegetation- or land-use types (LUT) to be sampled, respectively. Next it has to be decided if estimates will involve aboveground, i.e. over-mid- and under-storey, woody debris and coarse litter, or belowground, i.e. fine litter, soil organic carbon and roots, biomass and Cstocks.",\n        "Secondly, the size, shape and lifetime of the sampling plots have to be determined which range from 0.001ha to 1ha, squared to circular and from permanent to setting up just for one time measurements, respectively.",\n        "Furthermore it has to be decided if measurements will be conducted destructively or nondestructively. If measurements will be conducted destructively specific wood density, biomass and carbon stored in the different plant components can be determined exactly by logging and cutting the different plants and by subsequent freshand dry-weight determination. The generated values can be used for the development of site specific allometric models which relate biomass to simple measurable morphological plant features.",\n        "When deciding to use non-destructive measurements, biomass and C-stocks are estimated by combining relevant allometric models from secondary sources with non-destructive parameters measured on site.",\n        "Many different allometric models have been developed so far for primary forests, for secondary forests or plantation crops, according to plant morphology, e.g. shrubs, trees or lianas and according to climatic conditions, i.e. wet, moist, dry climates and to different mathematical regression approximations, e.g. power equations Y = a * X b where b typically has values of 2-3 (Smith and Brand 1984, Brown 1997, Araujo et al. 1999, Ketterings et al. 2001 and Chave et al. 2005, Henry et al. 2009).",\n        "For the allometric models mostly stem diameter at breast height (DBH) or plant height is either used as a single parameter or both parameters are combined (Brown 1997, Brown 2002). Litton and Kaufmann (2008) suggest in order to reduce measurement errors and enhance feasibility to rather use stem diameter as a single input parameter and Chave et al. (2005) emphasize wood specific gravity ρ (g/cm³) as third important parameter to correctly estimate AGB.",\n        "The abovementioned studies differ in their suggestions for the use of allometric models and conversion factors, such as which variables to be used and Root:Shoot ratios, respectively. Yet, most of the methods presented in the latter studies have been used as basis for the generally accepted methodology reports, Good Practice Guidance for Land Use and Land-Use Change and Forestry (LULUCF) and Guidelines for National Greenhouse Gas Inventories, on estimating biomass and carbon stocks, published by IPCC in 2003 and 2006, respectively."\n      ]\n    },\n    {\n      "heading": "5 Material and methods",\n      "content": []\n    },\n    {\n      "heading": "5.1 Study site description",\n      "content": []\n    },\n    {\n      "heading": "5.1.1 Geographical location",\n      "content": [\n        "The study was conducted in Chieng Khoi Commune, close to the district capital, the town of Yen Chau (Lat. 21° 18\' 0N, Long. 103° 54\' 0E, Fa ling Rain Genomics 2009), situated l Son La Province, Northwest Viet Nam (Figure 1). The commune covers a total area of 3189ha and an altitudinal range of 320 to 1600m.a.s.l.",\n        "Figure 1: Location of the study area, Chieng Khoi Commune. (Graphics and maps adapted from SFB 564The Uplands Program 2009).",\n        "<!-- image -->"\n      ]\n    },\n    {\n      "heading": "5.1.2 Population",\n      "content": [\n        "Mostly populated by people of Black Thai ethnic origin, Chieng Khoi Commune provides a living environment for 471 households with a total population of 2.201 inhabitants, distributed over six villages: Ban Put, Ban Me, Ban Tum, Ban Ngoang, Ban Hiem and Ban Dong. The average farm size is on 1.65ha per household and the land sufficiency rate is 0.35ha per person. Land area per household is highly fragmented into land use units that range between 0.03ha and 0.3ha. The main forms of use, in hectares can be characterized as crops, forest and farmstead, i.e. in hectares 0.99, 0.61 and 0.04, respectively (Dang et al. 2008)."\n      ]\n    },\n    {\n      "heading": "5.1.3 Land use",\n      "content": [\n        "Since the early 1950s the land cover of the once densely forested catchment area has changed drastically. As a result only the southern part covering an area of 929ha in high elevation with steep slopes and ridges is still covered by protected natural tropical semideciduous forest (Nguyen 2009). 1111ha of farmland are mainly located in the center and the northern up-and lowlands of the sub-catchment, which are intensively planted with paddy rice ( Oryza sativa ), maize ( Zea mays ), cassava ( Manihot esculenta Crantz ), maizecassava intercrop and vegetables. 75ha are used for aquaculture and an area of 263 ha was classified as unused or non-agricultural land in 2005 (Dang et al. 2008).",\n        "While maize and cassava are cultivated on the sloping fields and paddy rice in the valley bottoms, perennial crops are cultivated almost solely on upland slopes, hilltops or riverbanks.",\n        "Perennial land-use types mainly comprise timber and fruit tree plantations and forestry.",\n        "Crops and farm households, i.e. paddy rice cultivated on the lowlands of the Chieng Khoi watershed, depend on irrigation water from the artificial 40ha Chieng Khoi lake, established in 1969. Together with a spring, the lake is the main source of water during the dry season (Hertel 2007). In contrast, upland crops are grown under rainfed conditions."\n      ]\n    },\n    {\n      "heading": "5.1.4 Soils",\n      "content": [\n        "Dominant soil types include Cutanic Allisols and Stagnic Anthrosols and Gleysols in the low land paddy rice area (Clemens 2008; Stauss 2007, cited in Hertel 2007). Bedrock mainly comprise of limestone and schist (Bui Huy Hien et al.1995 cited in Wezel et al. 2002)."\n      ]\n    },\n    {\n      "heading": "5.1.5 Climate",\n      "content": [\n        "The mean annual precipitation in Chieng Khoi is 1500mm and follows a subtropical monsoonal distribution with a distinct rainy season from June to September (Figure 2). However, annual rainfall fluctuated from 1000 to 1700 mm over the years 2000 - 2006 as described by Hertel (2007). The mean annual temperature is 23.9°C with a minimum of 7°C during the cooler dry season and a maximum of 38°C during the warm rainy season (Nguyen 2009). This study was conducted during the rainy season (June to September 2009).",\n        "Figure 2 : Monthly rainfall and temperature averages 1998-2007 (Yen Chau weather station).",\n        "<!-- image -->"\n      ]\n    },\n    {\n      "heading": "5.1.6 Ecological zone",\n      "content": [\n        "According to FAO (2007) the region is classified as tropical mountain forest zone that merges northwards into the tropical moist forest zone (Figure 3).",\n        "Figure 3: Ecological zones map of Viet Nam (GFRA 2000, base map ESRI).",\n        "<!-- image -->"\n      ]\n    },\n    {\n      "heading": "5.2 Determination of representative Land-use types (LUTs)",\n      "content": [\n        "To obtain an overview of the present communal perennial LUTs an on-site preliminary survey was conducted in 2009, prior to the beginning of this study.",\n        "Results of this survey showed that perennial land-use types comprised timber plantations of Tectona grandis , Chukrasia tabularis , Pinus massoniana and Bamboo ssp . and fruit production of Mangifera indica , Dimocarpus longan , Musa spp. (basjoo and paradisiaca) , Citrus x grandis , Tamarindus sp. and Artocarpus sp.. Further, forested areas were classified into three types, namely protected natural tropical semi-deciduous forest found on steep slopes and ridges in the southern part of the commune, managed bamboo forest, found on the lakeside of the Chieng Khoi lake and grazed secondary forest found mostly on hill tops across the commune. Among these, the protected forest mostly comprises medium sized and large deciduous broad-leafed trees forming a dense stand canopy structure, while the managed bamboo and the grazed secondary forest vegetation mainly consist of small deciduous broad-leafed trees mixed with clusters of bamboo species Bambuseae sinospinosa (Chinese thorny bamboo).",\n        "Other, not explicitly surveyed LUTs were home-gardens, which mainly contained a small number of perennial species such as Citrus x grandis , Tamarindus indica, Carica papaya , Artocarpus spp. , hedgerows of Jatropha curcas and Manihot esculenta plots.",\n        "From the surveyed LUTs the following representative (according to their area of land coverage) perennial LUTs were determined for subsequent biomass estimation: protected natural tropical semi-deciduous forest (PF), grazed secondary (SF) and bamboo forest (BBF), plantations of Tectona grandis (TT), Mangifera spp. (MA), Dimocarpus longan (LON), Musa ssp. A posteriori, photographs of plants were used to botanically determine the surveyed plant species (personal communication, Langenberger 2009).",\n        "Diameter was calculated from the circumference measurements assuming that the crosssection of stems formed a circle.",\n        "Due to differences in carbon content (Kang et al. 2006) all AGB of destructively sampled mid-storey plants within the subplot 25m² were sepa rated into four components: stems + large branches ( ≥ 2cm), small branches + stems ( ≥ 1cm < 2cm), twigs (< 1cm) and leaves including petioles (expressed as foliage).",\n        "Destructively sampled Bamboo plants were separated into culm, leaves, large branches (diameter ≥ 1cm < 2cm), small branches (diameter ≥ 0.5cm < 1cm) and twigs (diameter < 0.5cm).",\n        "AGB of the species Melastoma sanguineum Sims and Chromolaena odorata (L.) into foliage, twigs (diameter < 1cm), branches and stems (diameter ≥ 1cm ) and into leaves, green twigs (diameter < 0.5cm), main stem and brown branches (diameter ≥ 0.5cm <1cm), respectively.",\n        "Subsequently, the total fresh weight (FW) of the different components was determined on site with an electronic scale (Ohaus CS 200), resolution 0.1 g. (2001).",\n        "<!-- image -->",\n        "Tree total height (HT) and tree height from the ground up to canopy base (HTc) of trees > 7m in total height were determined using an electronic, IR-laser beam based, handheld rangefinder (Nikon Forestry 550), with a built in inclinometer and a minimum distance to object requirement of 10m.",\n        "To avoid reflection of the sensitive laser beam by mid- and under-storey vegetation, trees were marked at 2m above ground. This 2m mark was then used as starting point for height measurements. Afterwards, the two meters from the tree base to the 2m mark were added to the measured height, to receive the total tree height or height to canopy, respectively. The calculation of the height lastly depended on the position of the surveyor, whether he was standing below, above or level to the 2m mark. Calculation of height, when measuring at level with, from below or above the 2m mark is depicted in Figure 11 a, b and c, respectively.",\n        "Figure height with a bamboo stick. 12 : Measuring tree",\n        "<!-- image -->",\n        "<!-- image -->",\n        "Figure 11 : Height measurement with rangefinder Nikon Forestry 550 at level ( a ), from below ( b ) and from above ( c ) the starting point (2m mark).",\n        "The top of the tree was found using the rangefinder\'s scan mode, moving back and forth across the top of the tree. In a series of branches at about the same angle upward, the one farthest away by the readings was taken for tree total height calculation. Often the best solution was to look for wider openings to get a better shot free from any intervening branches and to walk around the tree and look for scans from different angles. At minimum height measurements from two different positions were chosen to determine total tree height and height to canopy of an individual tree.",\n        "Height of trees less than 7m in total height and banana plants was measured by placing a straight bamboo stick marked with chalk at 25cm intervals parallel to the plant\'s main stem (Figure 12). Subsequently, the corresponding height values in line with the top of the canopy were read from the bamboo stick scale. Height values in between the scale intervals were estimated visually.",\n        "Coinciding with Snowdon et al. (2002) findings \'that ground based measurements are often obstructed by shape of crown and visibility\', within dense stands, due to reflection of the laser by branches of adjacent trees, the rangefinder could not be used. Thus, height estimations were made by visual appraisement; or representative trees of the outer margin of the stand were measured with the rangefinder to allow an average height estimation of the stand.",\n        "Width (WTcw) and length (LTcl) of the tree crown were measured successively with a measuring tape held by two persons in two perpendicular directions. The canopy margins were determined visually by looking upwards and reading the values of the measuring tape at the downward vertical elongation of the canopy edge (Figure 13). Subsequently, tree canopy diameter (dTc) was calculated as the average of WTcw and LTcl. The aim was, to develop own allometric models, relating total culm biomass to diameter and height.",\n        "After cutting the culms at base, total height was determined with a measuring tape. Subsequently aboveground components were separated into culm, leaves, large branches (diameter ≥ 1cm < 2cm), small branches (diameter ≥ 0.5cm < 1cm) and twigs (diameter < 0.5cm), according to Hunter and Junqi (2002). Total FW of each component was determined and subsamples, at minimum 2 x 300g were taken for oven dry weight determination (Figure 15a).",\n        "Due to their size, culms were treated differently. To determine dry weight, each culm was divided into three equal length sections and a piece of 30cm was cut thereof. Subsequently, for each 30cm piece fresh weight and dry weight was determined to receive an average dry-fresh weight correction factor.",\n        "A second approach was also tested, i.e. to determine biomass of the individual culms by determining each culms volume, which then was converted into biomass by multiplication by bamboo wood specific gravity ρ (g/cm³). Considering the hollow structure of the bamboo culms, successive culm diameter and wood thickness measurements starting at base were recorded by the meter (Figure 15b). To calculate the total volume it was assumed, that every section between circumference measurements of the bamboo culms had the shape of a right truncated cone. Subtracting the inner diameter derived volume from the outer consequently resulted in the approximate biomass volume per culm (Figure 15c). To determine bamboo culm biomass the following mathematical equation was used:",\n        "<!-- formula-not-decoded -->",\n        "[Volume of truncated cone = (height * π /3) * (R² + R * r + r²)]",\n        "<!-- image -->",\n        "(a)",\n        "Figure 15 : ( a) Fresh subsamples of bamboo leaves, twigs and stalks; ( wood; ( c) geometric description bamboo culm volume parameter measurements. b) measuring thickness of bamboo culm",\n        "<!-- image -->",\n        "<!-- image -->",\n        "To relate the individual culm biomass to clumping bamboo plants (found as part of the over-storey vegetation in the 100m² plots and comprising several culms), an average circumference of 5 representative culms was determined at breast height for each plant. Secondly, the total number of stems per plant was recorded and the average plant height was estimated by determining the height of 3-5 representative culms using the electronic rangefinder used for tree height measurements. Decomposition class density was derived from classifying the samples into three decomposition classes of dead wood: sound, rotten coarse and rotten fine, 0.69, 0.34 and 0.41 g/cm³, respectively (Nascimento and Laurance 2 002)."\n      ]\n    },\n    {\n      "heading": "5.4.6 Allometric models for bamboo, Melastoma sanguineum Sims and Chromolaena odorata (L.)",\n      "content": [\n        "In this study DBH (cm) and height (m) were used as variables for the bamboo model. Diameter at 30cm aboveground (diam30) as recommended by Gehring et al. (2008) and height were considered suitable for the model development for Melastoma sanguineum Sims . The model developed for Chromolaena odorata (L.) comprised stand height as independent parameter.",\n        "All three models were fit to the power function form: Y = a * X , where Y is biomass X b ; equals the independent variable [e.g. DBH (cm), diam30 (cm), height (m)] and a and b are regression coefficients.",\n        "SigmaPlot version 10.0 software was used for computing the different models."\n      ]\n    },\n    {\n      "heading": "5.5 Quantification of total AGB-, BGB and C-stocks",\n      "content": [\n        "To estimate the total AGB for each LUT, all non- and destructively determined biomass was summed up, i.e. oven-dry biomass of mid-, under-storey and coarse litter in the 25m², 1m² and 0.25m² subplots, respectively.",\n        "Secondly, for estimating AGB for over-storey biomass of trees within the 100m² main plots, this study used when available allometric species specific models (Appendix 1). If not available, the pan-tropical model by Brown (1997), Y = exp [1.996 + 2.32*ln (DBH)] suitable for the life zone dry in transition to moist (> 900mm), which mainly showed values close to the mean of all the models compared, was used to estimate AGB of trees. For bamboo, Chromolaena odorata (L.) and Melastoma sanguineum Sims stand estimates, models developed in this study were used (Table 3).",\n        "Wood specific gravity ρ of the sampled perennial over-storey vegetation, needed to estimate AGB using allometric models was derived from literature (Reyes et al. 1992) and from an online wood density database (WAC 2009). A default value for Asian trees, averaged from 428 species of 0.57 (0.4-0.69 g/cm³) from Reyes et al. (1992) was used only when values for wood density ρ were not available .",\n        "BGB for each LUT was estimated as ratio of AGB. A review of 165 studies, including trees ranging in age from 2 to 350 years, mean annual precipitation from 269 to 4000 mm, and mean annual temperature from 3.4 to 28°C by Cairns et al. (1997) suggests 0.26 as applicable for Root:Shoot and 0.7 for coarse : fine root ratios, respectively.",\n        "All estimates for AGB and BGB were up-scaled to main plot level and subsequently to hectare level according to the following equation:",\n        "Total BiomassLUT (Mg/ha) = AGB [ Σ (AGBsubplot * upscaling factor to 1ha)] + BGB [ Σ (AGBsubplot * upscaling factor to 1ha * 0.26)]",\n        "To quantify carbon stocks from the AGB- and BGB estimates a conversion factor of 0.5 as suggested by the IPCC (2007) was used.",\n        "Total C-stockLUT (Mg/ha) = 0.5 * (Total BiomassLUT)"\n      ]\n    },\n    {\n      "heading": "5.6 Up-scaling C-stock estimates to communal level",\n      "content": [\n        "In order to determine the present area covered for each surveyed land-use type and to give estimates of biomass and C-stocks on a communal level, a review of two land use maps of the Chieng Khoi commune from 1999 and the latest available map from 2007 (Giang 2003, Nguyen 2009) was conducted (Figure 18, 19).",\n        "The LU-map of 1999 by Giang categorizes the different LUTs into secondary forest, bamboo forest, natural forest, fruit tree, plantation forest (or timber tree), upland cultivation (cassava and maize), paddy rice, residential land and unused land. Due to the different classification approaches the LU-map by Nguyen of 1999 distinguishes only between upland cultivation (maize and cassava), trees (fruit and timber tree plantations and secondary and bamboo forest), paddy rice cultivation and natural forest. Hereby, as each farmstead is surrounded by a home-garden that by far exceeds the area occupied by the farmhouse itself residential land was incorporated into the tree category (Nguyen 2009).",\n        "Yet, the map of 1999 from Giang fitted the LUT categories defined in our study better. Thus as a first step we disassembled the aggregated tree category given in Nguyen\'s 1999 and 2007 map. This was done by calculating the percentage of area of the respective LUT given by Giang and applying these per cent ratios to the single tree category given by Nguyen. Secondly we assumed that the area occupied by each LUT in 1999 did change during 1999 to 2007 according to Nguyen\'s mapped area change 1999 to 2007. Lastly we combined the map classification area per cent ratios from step one with the variation (increase or decrease factor) of area according to Nguyen\'s maps from 1999-2007 to determine the present area covered for each surveyed land-use type.",\n        "To give estimates of total C-stock densities at a communal level for each LUT, each LUT\'s area was multiplied by the biomass respectively C-stocks estimated per hectare.",\n        "The sum of all perennial LUT C-stock densities resulted in the overall communal biomass and C-stock estimate for 2007.",\n        "Finally in order to obtain knowledge about possible future biomass and C-stock changes eight local farmers were interviewed qualitatively. One question included planned land use changes.",\n        "Six out of eight farmers stated that in the near future they would try to plant rubber trees ( Hevea brasiliensis ) on the area now occupied by plantation forests, such as fruit and",\n        "timber tree plantations. To see what would be the outcome of such a land-use change, this study developed one scenario of which 50% of the communal area now planted with timber and fruit trees was converted into rubber tree plantations. Values for AGB and carbon accumulation of rubber tree plantations were taken from Khun et al. (2008), who developed allometric models for stem volume and growth of Cambodian rubber tree plantations. To make the carbon stock values for rubber plantations comparable to other land-use systems they were time-averaged according to the method described in the next section. Stand ages for fruit, timber and rubber tree plantations were assumed to be 12 years. The total available area was assumed to be 365ha. Data to calculate time-averaged aboveground carbon stocks as shown in Table 2, were taken from Khun et al. (2008) and Manivong and Cramb (2008).",\n        "Figure 18: Land-use map of Chieng Khoi commune 1999 (adapted from Giang 2003).",\n        "<!-- image -->",\n        "Figure 19 : Land-use map of Chieng Khoi commune 2007 (adapted from Nguyen 2009). The depicted tree category comprises land-use systems of fruit, timber and secondary and bamboo forest. Home-gardens are common in the Chieng Khoi commune and cover most of the farmstead surrounding area. The sum of both phases equals the rotation time (Tr) for the entire fruit tree system. Figure 20 gives a schematic overview of two successive fruit tree plantations.",\n        "Figure 20 : Schematic of aboveground C losses and re-accumulation after forest clearing and establishment of fruit tree plantation. Maximum carbon sequestered during establishment phase (Cmax ), time to reach its maximum C (Tmax), rotation time (Tr), carbon stored in primary forest (Cforest), time at start of rotation (T0). Adapted from ABS (1999).",\n        "<!-- image -->",\n        "When looking at timber tree plantations however, the establishment phase is the time until the maximum (Tmax) timber biomass accumulation occurs, or in other words the preferred stem diameter is reached and a subsequent logging will take place. In the Chieng Khoi commune timber was mainly logged prior to this maximum. Therefore the second phase, the production phase as given for fruit trees was assumed to be zero in our study\'s",\n        "calculations for timber trees. The same was assumed to be true of the surveyed forests. Hence, with regard to timber tree plantations and the surveyed forests the establishment time equals the rotation time (Figure 21).",\n        "Figure 21 : Schematic of aboveground C losses and re-accumulation after forest clearing and establishment of timber tree plantations. Maximum carbon sequestered establishment phase (Cmax), time to reach its maximum C (Tmax), rotation time (T ), carbon stored in primary forest (C r forest ), time at start of rotation or forest reestablishment (T0). Adapted from ABS (1999).",\n        "<!-- image -->",\n        "Other required parameters to calculate time-averaged C-stocks, i.e. the maximum carbon sequestered (Cmax) in each LUT per rotation, the time for each LUT to reach its maximum C (Tmax) and the rotation time of each system (Tr) were determined by interviewing local farmers and by our study\'s field measurements. Lastly, the carbon accumulation rate per year (Ic), i.e. the linear carbon accumulation during the establishment phase was calculated using the following equation:",\n        "<!-- formula-not-decoded -->",\n        "I = carbon accumulation rate per year (MgC/ha/year), C c max = maximum carbon sequestered in land-use system, Tmax = time were the land-use system reaches Cmax.",\n        "Note that with regard to the aforementioned, the carbon accumulation rate per year (Ic) for timber plantations and the surveyed forests was considered to be equal to the annual timeaveraged carbon stock accumulation.",\n        "For the calculation of time averaged carbon stocks of fruit tree plantations according to ASB (1999), the time averaged C-stock for the establishment phase (Cta1) of fruit tree plantations was calculated as follows:",\n        "<!-- formula-not-decoded -->",\n        "I = carbon accumulation rate per year (MgC/ha/year), Cta1 = time averaged C-stock for the establishment c phase, Tmax = time were the land-use system reaches Cmax.",\n        "Secondly, the time averaged C-stock for the production phase Cta2 was assumed to be equal to Cmax. Subsequently, the time-averaged C for the entire rotation was calculated as the weighted average of the two phases:",\n        "<!-- formula-not-decoded -->",\n        "Cr = The time-averaged C for the entire rotation (MgC/ha), Cta1 = time-averaged C-stock for the establishment phase, Cta2 = time-averaged C-stock for the production phase, Tmax = time were the land-use system reaches Cmax, Tr = rotation time.",\n        "Lastly, annual time-averaged carbon stock accumulation was calculated by dividing the time-averaged C for the entire rotation by the respective rotation time.",\n        "<!-- formula-not-decoded -->",\n        "I ta = annual time-averaged C-accumulatio, Cr = The time-averaged C for the entire rotation, Tr = rotation time",\n        "Table 2 summarizes the defined values for Ic, Cmax, Tmax and Tr and gives the annual timeaveraged C-accumulation and time-averaged C sequestration over the entire rotation according to land-use type.",\n        "Table 2 : Defined values for annual aboveground carbon accumulation rate (Ic), maximum C sequestered (Cmax), time for each LUT to reach its maximum (Tmax) and the rotation time of the entire system (Tr); The statistical analysis was done with the program R, version 2.8.1 (www.r-project.org). In addition, the packages multcomp, version 1.1-2 and mvtnorm, version 0.9-8 (http://cran.r-project.org/) were used. The data was tested for a normal distribution with the Shapiro test. If the data was not normal distributed, it was log-transformed. After that, the influence of the category (i.e. timber, fruit tree plantations and forests) on the content of carbon was analyzed with an analysis of variance (ANOVA). If the category had an significant effect using an error level of 5%, Tukey\'s all-pair comparisons was appli ed to find out which groups differed significantly."\n      ]\n    },\n    {\n      "heading": "6 Results",\n      "content": []\n    },\n    {\n      "heading": "6.1 Allometric models",\n      "content": []\n    },\n    {\n      "heading": "6.1.1 Bamboo, Chromolaena odorata (L.) and Melastoma sanguineum Sims",\n      "content": [\n        "Two allometric equations for height and DBH as independent variables were developed from biomass determined by 6 destructively harvested bamboo culms. A comparison of the two models showed that the equation using height as input variable over- or underestimated the destructively determined culms biomass by ±17%. 2006. Thus a clear distinction, between tropical dry or moist zone is not given. Yet, this distinction has a large influence on the estimation of AGB results. In this context, this study tested eight pantropical models from Brown (1997), Ponce-Hernandez (2004) and Chave et al. (2005). References of the different pan-tropical allometric models to estimate the over-storey AGB are given in Appendix 1.",\n        "The results showed a wide range of over-storey AGB estimates for the surveyed LUT plots. In particular the distinction of the climatic conditions (moist or dry) and in addition the choice of input parameters (i.e. DBH. height and wood specific gravity), had an influence on the biomass estimates. Moreover, the range of the biomass estimates increased with higher input values for DBH of the respective measured trees.",\n        "Within the category of surveyed forests the largest differences showed when comparing all models for the protected natural forest (ø DBH = 12 .4cm) with a minimum AGB estimate of 207.18 Mg/ha and a maximum of 391.68 Mg/ha, i.e. a divergence of 89% (Figure 23a). Biomass found in under-storey was 0.42, 0.24 and 0.42 Mg/ha for the grazed secondary, the bamboo and protected natural forest. The mid-storey vegetation contained biomass of 4.64, 3.60 and 5.77 Mg/ha for the grazed secondary, the bamboo and protected natural forest. Living over-storey biomass estimates were 81.64, 299.09 and 207.18 Mg/ha for the grazed secondary, the bamboo and protected natural forest, respectively. In addition, dead wood accounted for 0.43, 0.48 and 215.18 Mg/ha for the grazed secondary, the bamboo and protected natural forest. For the calculation of carbon densities this study used time-averaged C-stock values and assumed stand ages of 12 years for all timber and fruit tree plantations. In addition, for calculations of communal C-stocks this study used category-averaged values for the land-use type categories fruit and timber tree plantations, i.e. the average of all determined C-stocks of the surveyed fruit and timber tree systems, respectively. Belowground C-stocks were calculated by using a conversion factor of 0.26. Subsequently belowground C-stocks were added to the respective aboveground carbon stock to receive the total C-stock.
