The distinction of the climatic zone and the independent variables (DBH, height and wood density) used for the regression model, have a big influence on the results. Moreover, the range of application per allometric model is of importance (Brown 2002, Chave et al. 2004, Litton and Kauffman 2008).",\n        "Thus pan-tropical models should be applied with utmost care or be replaced by available site- and species-specific models which can provide more accurate biomass estimates (Clark and Clark 2000, Litton and Kauffman 2008, Náar 2009). v",\n        "However, if only few measurements are available on site, Chave et al. (2004) recommend caution and suggest to give preference to allometric models based on regional or pantropical compilations. According to their study, only equations based on a minimum sample of 100 individuals should be used, preferably including wood specific gravity as predictor of biomass, when ever possible.",\n        "There is clearly a need for more primary data to improve AGB estimates. This is in particular true, as most existing biomass regression equations are based on inventory data from the 60s and 80s (Brown 2002). Evermore, when looking at different sources of literature used in this study, there is a need for a compilation of all existing allometric equations ever developed. Lastly, not only in order to compare recent data with such from the past, but also to give answer to the question raised by Brown (2002), whether allometric relationships of single species have changed over time. Since then, an increases or decreases in carbon storage as a result of environmental changes (e.g., increasing concentrations of CO2, N deposition, climatic change or pollution) might have altered plant growing patterns (Houghton 2008).",\n        "Nevertheless, once the regression has been validated, the use of allometric models in comparison to destructive measurements has many advantages. According to Hairiah et al. (2001) estimation is rapid, a much larger area can be sampled and it reduces the sampling error encountered with the destructive method. Furthermore non-destructive measurements do not disturb the ecosystem under investigation and are less labor intensive (Gehring et al. 2008)."\n      ]\n    },\n    {\n      "heading": "7.2 Allometric equation for bamboo",\n      "content": [\n        "To evaluate the allometric model developed in this study for the estimation of AGB of individual bamboo culms, we used an often cited allometric model from Priyadarsini (1998, cited in Hairiah et al. 2001), which was developed in Indonesia. Figure 35a shows the divergences of our and Priyadarsini\'s model from the actual destructively determined biomass per culm.",\n        "At small diameter classes differences between the biomass estimates of the two models where small. With increasing diameter the model from Priyadarsini underestimated the destructively determined amount of culm biomass by up to 13%. One reason for this observed difference might be the limited diameter range of the model from Priyadarsini, which was 3-7cm. Yet, the bamboo diameter range of the bamboo culms harvested in this study was 2.75 to 9.5cm. Therefore this study supports the suggestion by Chave et al. (2004) that only models covering the surveyed parameter range should be used.",\n        "Determined biomass",\n        "40",\n        "30",\n        "20",\n        "10",\n        "0",\n        "Priyadarsini (1998)",\n        "Own developed model",\n        "4",\n        "0",\n        "a.",\n        "2",\n        "6",\n        "8",\n        "10",\n        "DBH (cm)",\n        "Figure 35 : ( a ) Culms biomass estimates of this study\'s model and the model from Priyadarsini (1998) compared to destructively determined biomass, ( b ) Comparison of biomass estimates non-destructively sampled bamboo culms given by this study\'s model and the model from Priyadarsini (1998).",\n        "<!-- image -->",\n        "Moreover, when looking at the biomass estimates in Figure 35b of bamboo plants measured non-destructively in the grazed secondary and the bamboo forest, the difference between the two models showed clearly even in small diameter classes. Whether our model or the model by Priyadarsini over- or underestimates the actual amount of biomass could be only determined by further destructive measurements (Clark and Clark 2000).",\n        "Biomass (kg)",\n        "When additionally taking into account allometric equations that include size structure relationships such as DBH to height, as suggested by Litton and Kauffman (2008), to distinguish the applicability of a general allometry, this study found no correlation to the plant structure relationship given by Priyadarsini (y (height) = 1.4527*DBH 0.963 ). A comparison showed an underestimation of height related to DBH by 60-72%. Our model was y = 2.5385*DBH 0.8466 (R² = 0.99 and SE = 0.5885). Thus the divergence between both models might also be due to bamboo species-specific differences.",\n        "All in all, these findings support the statements by Clark and Clark (2000) calling for caution, when applying generalized models, as they often differ from site- and speciesspecific models and therefore can lead to an over- or underestimation of biomass. Thus to minimize the error of biomass estimates from the choice of the regression model, site and species specific models as done in this study should be used whenever applicable."\n      ]\n    },\n    {\n      "heading": "7.3 Allometric equation for Melastoma sanguineum Sims",\n      "content": [\n        "Studies giving allometric models for mid-storey vegetation are scarce. A study by Smith and Brand (1984) that reviewed and summarized several studies on allometric equations for north-American shrubs and herbs shows that often diameter at base or at 15cm above ground level were used as independent variables to predict mid-storey biomass. Litton and Kauffman (2008) also use the diameter at base for their regression models for Dodonaea viscosa , a widespread woody plant on Hawaii.",\n        "However, as regressions for basal diameter to total shrub biomass resulted in low fit in this study, we developed an allometric equation for Melastoma sanguineum Sims using stem diameter at 30cm aboveground as predictor for biomass. Hereby, our model supports the findings of Gehring et al. (2008), who suggest using diameter at 30cm as independent variable to determine biomass of shrubs and small trees.",\n        "Yet, there is another reason in favor of using diameter at 30cm. As experienced in this study, it can be practically far more difficult to clearly distinguish and measure the diameter at base of a shrub. For example, during this study\'s field measurements irregular shapes at base were common due to adventive roots and stem bulking, whereas at 30 cm aboveground stems had more regular shapes.",\n        "Therefore, this study supports the appeal of Gehring et al. (2008) to develop more allometric equations for small and mid-sized woody vegetation based on diameter at 30cm height."\n      ]\n    },\n    {\n      "heading": "7.4 Allometric equation for Chromolaena odorata (L.)",\n      "content": [\n        "The invasive perennial weed is listed among the 100 plant species of the \'World\'s Worst Weeds\' invaders (GISD 2006). In fallows Chromolaena odorata (L.) ceases due to light competition after 10-20 years.",\n        "Yet, compared to other weeds, Chromolaena odorata (L.) has a high nutrient content (N 3.32% and 0.16% P) and a favorable chemical composition, which makes this plant a good source for green mulch. The use as organic fertilizer thereby would have two positive effects, spreading of the weed could be kept in check and the use of inorganic fertilizer inputs reduced (Abdullah and Puspitasari 2007).",\n        "To provide an easily applicable biomass estimation of the herbaceous perennial Chromolaena odorata (L.) , this study developed an allometric model based on average stand height per square meter. Moreover, the amount of biomass found in litter, mid- and under-storey vegetation of timber and fruit tree plantations is highly dependent on management practices.",\n        "According to Brown (1997), the amount of biomass found in under-storey vegetation and litter compared to AGB can be as low as 3%, e.g. in primary forests, but also as high as 30% in secondary disturbed forest stands. The findings in our study were on average 6.45%, 4.20% and 7.81% in forests, timber and fruit tree plantations, and are therefore within the lower percentage range given by Brown (1997).",\n        "While the lowest percentage of litter compared to AGB was found in our study in the protected natural forest with 2.86%, the highest amount of litter was found in the grazed secondary forest, with 13.95%. Hereby the proportion of coarse litter sampled in the latter made up 8.63%, i.e. 8.24 Mg/ha in biomass. This value corresponds with the mean of 8.3 Mg/ha for litter biomass of various secondary tropical Australasian forests (Woldendorp et al. 2002).",\n        "Yet, the high amount of litter found in this study\'s secondary forest might be due to the high proportion of bamboo leaves present in the coarse litter fraction. Christanty et al. (1996, cited in Hunter and Jungi 2002) also found high amounts of coarse litter in surveyed secondary forests and related their findings to the high silica content of bamboo leaves. The resulting slow decomposition rates thus lead to an accumulation of biomass in litter. A study by Liu et al. (2000) on leaf litter decomposition of broad-leafed trees and bamboo in a montane moist evergreen broad-leaved forest in south-west China also showed decomposition times of 2.5 years for bamboo leaves as compared to 0.50-0.64 for the broad-leafed tree litter.",\n        "Lastly, our findings do not confirm the suggestion made by Nascimento and Laurance (2002) who claim that, due to their findings of biomass quantities concerning large trees, overall biomass can be estimated just by measuring large trees in the field. The rest of the biomass, i.e. mid-under-storey and coarse litter, they argue can be calculated by a correction factor of 22.2%. When using this factor for litter, under- and mid-storey biomass the total AGB of our study would have been overestimated by 8-20%. However, this divergence might be explained by the different climatic zone (central Amazonian rainforest 1900-3500mm) in which the study of Nascimento and Laurance (2002) was conducted."\n      ]\n    },\n    {\n      "heading": "7.6 Biomass and C-stock estimates for over-storey vegetation biomass",\n      "content": [\n        "Even though there can be a large variation between results, depending on the allometric equation used, most AGB was accumulated in the over-storey vegetation, i.e. 85 to 97%.",\n        "These findings coincide with many studies giving percentages of over-story biomass, e.g. 81.9% (Nascimento and Laurance 2002), 81% (Henry et al. 2009) and 92.7-94.1% (Clark and Clark 2000)."\n      ]\n    },\n    {\n      "heading": "7.7 Estimates of total AGB and C-stock densities for timber and fruit tree plantations",\n      "content": [\n        "The amount of AGB and carbon stocks in plantations mainly depends on management practices, i.e. on the one hand on pruning intervals and on the other on the overall tree stand densities and thinning. Moreover, stocks of plant available soil nutrients and water availability can explain a significant fraction of the variation in AGB (Albrecht and Kandji 2003, Clark and Clark 2000, Laurance et al. 1999 cited in Chave et al. 2004).",\n        "Thus, this study\'s biomass estimates of 29.29 , 20.28 and 71.84 Mg/ha for the surveyed extensive longan, mixed fruit tree and mango plantations with stand densities of 400 to 700 trees/ha can only be considered as site-specific estimates. These values could be much higher under more intense cultivation. For example stand densities of over 1200 trees per hectare as described by Chadha and Pal (1993) would result in higher biomass values for the mango plantation.",\n        "Therefore, when looking at biomass estimates of plantations, one has to consider whether the plantation was set up exclusively to sequester carbon or with a multifunctional purpose, i.e. to provide additional income opportunities and services such grazing-ground for cattle. Lastly it has to be kept in mind that soil fertility and water availability have a large influence on the amount of biomass and carbon stored in land-use types, such as timber and fruit tree plantations.",\n        "Nevertheless, the overall significant higher carbon accumulation of timber plantations compared to fruit tree plantations, as shown in this study\'s results, give an indication to choose timber over fruit trees in order to sequester the highest amount of carbon in a short time period of 12 years.",\n        "Yet, there are large differences in biomass between timber species. As shown, the pine tree plantation quickly accumulates biomass and carbon, while Chukrasia tabularis and teak trees rather had low to moderate growth rates. Thus, one might be easily tempted to conclude that pine trees are the best option to sequester carbon.",\n        "Nair et al. (2009), however, argue that one should consider that apparently rapid accumulation of carbon in fast-growing species may be deceptive, as wood specific density of pine trees is relatively low when compared to teak and Chukrasia tabularis . Products made out of the different wood types often reflect this circumstance. High value products, that have lifetimes ranging from decades to centuries, e.g. boles for farm house construction are made from teak or Chukrasia tabularis , while short-lived products like packaging cases or paper are made from pine wood. The release of CO2 to the atmosphere from products manufactured from pine wood might therefore occur much earlier than that of teak or Chukrasia tabularis .",\n        "Thus, even though differences in biomass and carbon accumulation between the three surveyed timber plantations were shown, when making the final decision on which tree species to use for reforestation projects, one should also consider the subsequent usage and price development on the local timber market.",\n        "Mixed tree plantations, including fast and slow growing tree species, that provide harvestable wood at different times as suggested by Nair et al. (2009) might be a good combination, compensating for fluctuations of demand on the timber market. Skog and Nicholson (1998) suggest in order to increase the longevity of wood products to shift the product mix to a greater proportion of lignin-containing products, which decay more slowly when thrown away, to increase product recycling or to increase product use-life in general."\n      ]\n    },\n    {\n      "heading": "7.8 Estimates of total AGB and C-stock densities for forests",\n      "content": [\n        "The biomass (and carbon stocks) density estimates for forests, i.e. for the grazed secondary, bamboo and protected natural forest were 95.38 (47.69), 307.39 (153.69). and 435.06 (217.53) Mg/ha (MgC/ha), respectively.",\n        "Expressed as percentage the bamboo and grazed secondary forest had 70% and 22% of the amount of biomass accumulated in the protected natural forest, Houghton and Hackler (1999) state that secondary forests have approximately 70% of biomass accumulated in primary forests. Aboveground vegetation feeds this important carbon pool by the input of litter, root exudation and root turnover. This becomes evermore obvious when considering that 45% of all assimilated C is transferred below ground to roots and the mycorrhizae in tropical forests (Malhi and Grace 2000). Thus \'soils in undisturbed tropical rain forests contain enormous amounts of carbon derived from fallen leaves, twigs and buried roots that can bind to soil particles and remain there for 1000 years or more\\" (Carrere and González 2000).",\n        "A review of 160 studies by Cairns (1997) showed that BGB can be estimated by AGB (Brown 2002). An average root to shoot ratio of 0.26 (0.18-0.30) showed no significant variation with regard to latitudal zone, soil texture or tree height (Brown 2002).",\n        "However, no standardized methods to determine BGB in forest ecosystems exist so far (Brown 2002). Furthermore, \'the biomass of roots varies considerably among tropical forests depending mainly upon climate and soil characteristics\' (Brown and Lugo 1984, Sanford and Cuevas 1996 cited in Brown 1997). An estimated range given by Brown (1997) was 4-230% of total AGB in mature forests, showing the wide range of variability across ecozones and vegetation types.",\n        "Due to limited time and labor capacities our study used the ratio of 0.26 proposed by Brown (2002, s. above) to estimate root biomass from AGB estimates and to subsequently calculate the total overall biomass and C-stock densities for each land-use type (sum of above- and below-ground vegetation biomass).",\n        "The ranges of carbon stored in BGB of the surveyed timber, fruit tree plantations and forests, were 6.12-18.83, 2.19-6.56 and 12.40-56.56 MgC/ha, respectively.",\n        "Since this study did not destructively measure belowground biomass such as fine and coarse roots, nor soil organic matter (SOM) respectively soil organic carbon, this study makes the following hypotheses that might be taken up by future studies to be verified.",\n        "The amount of soil organic carbon in banana plantations decreases with cultivation time, as the observed management of bare soils enhances soil respiration, as litter input is relatively low and as pseudostems of mature banana stools are regularely removed for pig and cattle feed, leaving only the roots as input to the belowground C-pool.",\n        "Secondly, slow litter decomposition rates observed in the grazed secondary forest and in the pine plantation lead to a significant increase of the organic matter layer of the topsoil and over time to an increase in below-ground C-stocks.",\n        "Lastly, this study assumes that the input to the soil carbon pool is positively correlated to the amount of input from the litter and root exudates. These amounts of inputs are in turn directly linked to the AGB. Thus, there should be a correlation between SOC and AGB, or in other words, the higher the amount of AGB, the higher the SOC content. This leads to",\n        "the third hypothesis that the ranking of C-stocks between land-uses as presented in this study can be likewise applied to the SOC content of the observed land-use types.",\n        "Yet, clearly more studies of root biomass and their relationship with other factors such as AGB, climate and soil, are needed. Ignoring these forest components can underestimate the total biomass of a forest significantly, Moreover, the dynamic connection between above-, BGB and SOC needs to be further examined. The goal for future studies should be to give estimates of total carbon stocks including all three components, above-, BGB and SOC. However, more logistically and economically feasible methods and approaches must be developed to estimate this significant quantity of biomass and SOC, respectively (Brown 2002)."\n      ]\n    },\n    {\n      "heading": "7.10 Limitations of used and tested methodology",\n      "content": [\n        "Certainly, a larger number of sample plots could improve the validity of our results. However, the methodology used gives trends and relationships that confirm other studies with regards to biomass and C-stocks.",\n        "Besides the methods used for the main sample plots this study also tested some methods to determine biomass. Firstly, the volumetric biomass determination of bamboo culms, secondly the determination of root biomass of Melastoma sanguineum Sims with a root corer and the fractional branch analysis approach given by van Noordwijk and Mulia (2002) to non-destructively determine AGB of trees. The results of all three methods are not shown in this study, as the results were not representative, did not give any good result or were just not feasible with regard to available labor and time resources. To give some hints for further studies this study briefly gives some explanations for latter assumptions.",\n        "The volumetric biomass determination of individual bamboo culms resulted in an overestimation of total culm biomass by 2-8%. This divergence was less than the divergence of -13% shown when using the allometric model from Priyadarsini (1998). Yet, the limited time and labor resources restricted the cutting and measuring of culms thickness at every meter to only three culms in total. Clearly a general statement whether this method is valid has to be verified by a larger sample size. This study suggests the methodology to be applicable, if biomass has to be determined destructively and no possibility for dry weight determination is given. Nevertheless only if time and labor resources are available this approach should be used.",\n        "Determining the BGB of the shrub Melastoma sanguineum Sims by using a root corer did not give good results. Proof to this assumption was given by the subsequent excavation of the shrub\'s total rooting system. The excavation showed that due to the structure of the root system the soil cores did not contain any roots from the shrub itself. Therefore this study suggests using the root corer method only after pre-testing the spatial horizontal and vertical distribution of the root system of the designated plant to be sampled. Thereafter the spatial arrangement of root cores can be allocated.",\n        "The functional branch analysis was also tried as suggested by van Noordwijk and Mulia (2002). However, the feasibility was not as assumed. Firstly, compared to measurements of DBH and height the measurement of successive diameters on various branches is highly labor- and time-intensive and secondly is hampered by the accessibility of larger trees. Therefore this study contradicts the claims of van Noordwijk and Mulia (2002) and characterizes this method to be not suitable for an easy and fast biomass and carbon stocks assessment.",\n        "In sum determining carbon and biomass stocks on communal level is feasible with the methods used in this study, even though on site determination of biomass is labor and time consuming. Therefore it has to be well planed. On large scale measurement approaches field methods will have to be combined with satellite data in order to be overall feasible (Goetz et al. 2009)."\n      ]\n    },\n    {\n      "heading": "7.11 Land use change",\n      "content": [\n        "Nguyen (2009) showed in her study that in Chieng Khoi commune the area occupied by protected natural forest and tree plantations decreased between 1993-2007 by 19% and 13%, respectively. On the other hand the area of upland crops increased by 230%. Assuming a totally forested commune before 1954, the decrease in natural forested area was 70%. However, the increase in tree plantations and afforested areas of 44% of the total commune reduced this drastic deforestation rate of 70% to a net decrease in total forested area of 36%.",\n        "Therefore we assume that a progressive continuous downward trend exists in natural forested area with a predominant exchange of this cleared area with annual cropping systems. It remains unclear, whether this trend can be slowed down, halted or even",\n        "reversed. Two incentives for a trend shift could be the highly promoted establishment of rubber tree plantations (own interview data), and the implementation of CDM projects.",\n        "However this trend is observed nation wide. During the time period from 1943-2000 Viet Nam\'s natural forested area decreased by 44% (14.3 to 9.4million ha) whereat plantation forest area increased from zero to 1.47million ha in 2000. The net decrease in total forested area was 33.2% (Trieu 2004).",\n        "Despite the assumption that reforestation projects will increase in area of land coverage in the near future, there still remains the negative connotation that, when natural forests are cut \'the trees\' roots decay and soil is disrupted, releasing the carbon dioxide. Centuries would have to pass until newly planted trees built up such a reservoir underground\\" (Carrere and González (2000)."\n      ]\n    },\n    {\n      "heading": "7.12 Applicability of clean development mechanism (CDM) in the Chieng",\n      "content": []\n    },\n    {\n      "heading": "Khoi commune",\n      "content": [\n        "CDM projects can provide advantages for poor farmers in remote areas found in the communes of northwest Viet Nam. This is in particular true when taking into consideration that, there is no need for physical transport, secondly no quality differences exist, i.e. one molecule of CO2 sequestered is the same all over the globe and thus the difficulties of getting market access because of certain quality standards as often the case with agricultural products would drop out. Moreover even small financial benefits gained from participation in CDM projects can make a big difference in low or non-income communities (Nair et al. 2009). Nevertheless, it has to be noted that CDM projects themselves are subject to certain standards, such as the gold standard which considers the projects sustainability and leakage effects (UNFCCC 2004).",\n        "To determine the feasibility and outcomes of a CDM project within the Chieng Khoi commune, this study first determines niches of the surveyed land-uses that have the potential to increase AGB C-stocks, secondly gives estimates of the transaction costs of a CDM project, discusses different scenarios and finally evaluates the feasibility and outcomes with regard to other factors that make a CDM project beneficial for local farmers and the international community.",\n        "In Chieng Khoi commune options that might have the potential for an additional potential to increase aboveground C-stocks could be the reforestation of upland fields, now cropped",\n        "with maize and cassava. Secondly, the intensification of extensively cultivated fruit tree plantations, by increasing overall stand densities. Thirdly, replacing all teak and Chukrasia tabularis stands by tree species with higher annual C-accumulation rates, such as pine plantations. However, in order to test and evaluate the feasibility of a CDM project and avoid any competition with food production, the utilization of unused communal land might be considered a suitable first option.",\n        "For a crediting period of 20 years Locatelli and Pedroni (2006, cited in Henry et al. 2009) estimated total transaction costs of US$ 102.000 for project design (US$ 20.000), validation and registration (US$ 20.000), monitoring (US$ 2000), and verification costs (US$ 15.000 every five years). Assuming a minimum market price of US$ 10 per Mg of CO2, the minimum CDM project size would have to store in 20 years a minimum of 10.200 Mg of CO2 or approximately 2800 Mg of C (Conversion factor 12/44 by molecular weights).",\n        "As a first scenario, we assume that unused communal land only covered by small shrubs and grasses is reforested with pine trees, which when considering time-averaged data accumulate 6 MgC/ha annually in AGB as shown in this study\'s results. Thus a minimum area of approximately 24 ha would be needed for the establishment of our 20-year CDM project. Yet, areas of unused communal land are scarce in the commune of Chieng Khoi. In the 1999 land-use map of Giang (2003) the total area classified as unused land was only 16ha.",\n        "Therefore we will look at another example scenario. Hereby we assume that mango trees which as shown in our results accumulate 1.8 MgC/ha/yr (time-averaged) are replaced by teak trees which accumulate 3.8 MgC/ha/yr (time-averaged). The resulting difference of additional accumulated C of 2 MgC/ha/yr in AGB would require a minimum area of 70ha. Considering that the agricultural land in the Chieng Khoi commune is highly fragmented into average farm sizes of 1.6ha per household, a minimum of approximately 45 households in the Chieng Khoi commune would have to cooperate. However as in the case of our example, if only the tree plantation area per household, i.e. 0.6ha, was used for the implementation of the CDM project, 115 households would have to act jointly.",\n        "Looking at the number of households it becomes obvious that there would have to be a framework that links the single households together. Furthermore this \'middle man\' would need to be capable to understand and transfer the complex modalities and rules of a CDM",\n        "project. Locatelli and Pedroni (2003) call this type of project framework \'umbrella projects\', in which a national or local organization deals with the international carbon market and manages at the same time many small plantation projects (Figure 36).",\n        "Figure 36 : Umbrella project framework (Locatelli and Pedroni 2003).",\n        "<!-- image -->",\n        "Nevertheless as Locatelli and Pedroni (2003) state, these \'advantages of umbrella projects are not without prerequisites that must be analysed during the project design phase. Among these are the following:",\n        "- · The organisation must be able to manage a number of small projects without increasing the transaction costs too much.",\n        "- · A loan or incentive that is sufficiently attractive for the small stakeholders must be dispensed and at the same time must ensure the financial equilibrium of the whole project.",\n        "- · The organisation must guarantee the long-term stakeholders through contracts. commitment of its small",\n        "- · The organisation requires to have at its disposal a sufficient buffer of areas in its own hands or in those of potential future stakeholders in which new forests can be planted for replacing carbon stocks that might disappear in the future because of fires, pests, harvest or farmers that decided to quit the project.\'",\n        "However, when considering the case of 45 households, the income from maize production would have to be added as opportunity costs. In the time period 2007-2009 the regional mean income from maize production per household was $US 728.97/ha (613.68-938.44) or $US 625/ha when excluding the exceptional high prizes of 2008 (Ufer 2009).",\n        "Considering the opportunity costs of the scenario of the 45 households, i.e. transaction costs and the income loss of $US 625 from abandoning maize production, to reach break even we calculated the minimum price to be US$ 65MgC.",\n        "The income generated from mango fruit production compared to maize production in Chieng Khoi commune is marginal. Thus this study neglects the loss of income for the abandoned mango fruit production for further calculations.",\n        "Considering the latter, the minimum price to cover the transaction costs of 115 households would obviously have to be $US10. Yet an increase in the price for one mega gram of carbon to $US 20, 50 or 65 would result in annual income gains of $US 45, 180 and 245 for each of the 115 participating farming household.",\n        "Taking the actual price of 20 US$ for one mega gram of carbon (EU ETS 2009) only the scenario which includes 115 households would be cost-effective.",\n        "Nair et al. (2009) give in their study an example for an agroforestry project from the highlands of Peru. A price of $US 50/MgC showed overall positive effects on farmers income and on sustainable ecosystem management and services. Increasing the financial benefit to $US 100/MgC raised the per capita income by up to 15% on farms with steeply sloped fields and reduced regional poverty by 9%.",\n        "In sum, as CDM projects seem promising to generate new income as shown above they highly depend on the price of carbon. Costs for registration and monitoring have to be covered. Furthermore, in order to be sustainable there has to be a balance between pure carbon sequestration and preservation of biodiversity. There will be a need for improved timber and non-timber product markets and lastly all these benefits will only be feasible when farmers are willing to participate (Locatelli and Pedroni 2003. Grace 2004, Nikolic et al. 2008, Nair et al. 2009).",\n        "It is beyond the scope of this study to give policy recommendations. Further studies in particular on behalf of the socio-economic sector need to be conducted. None of the interviewed farmers in this study had any idea about climate change. Extension services will have to inform farmers about climate change and the importance of their participation. Moreover studies including the soil carbon storage pool have to be conducted. Not until then a thorough estimate of the applicability of a communal CDM project in Chieng Khoi commune can be given. Yet we suggest that agroforestry systems could be a solution as",\n        "these land-use systems seem to have the most promising ability for carbon storage, biodiversity and watershed protection, and keeping of scenic beauty, as done in a project in Costa Rica (Locatelli and Pedroni 2003)."\n      ]\n    },\n    {\n      "heading": "7.13 C-stock of rubber tree ( Hevea brasiliensis ) plantations",\n      "content": [\n        "According to information given by local farmers on future land-use changes, in foreseeable future rubber tree ( Hevea brasiliensis ) plantations will be established in Chieng Khoi commune. In this context in order to give an outlook on the outcome in terms of carbon sequestration, this study developed a scenario (calculated results not shown) in which half of the communal area now covered by stands of fruit and timber trees (730ha) was converted into rubber tree plantations.",\n        "When looking at the overall outcome of our scenario, calculations showed a decrease of 3% in total communal carbon stocks.",\n        "However, outcomes on behalf of communal C-stocks differed, when comparing timber und fruit tree plantations separately to rubber tree plantations. Hereby, solely replacing timber plantations by rubber tree plantations reduced C-stocks all the more. Yet the replacement of fruit tree based land-use systems by rubber tree plantations showed an increase in overall communal C-stocks.",\n        "Thus when exclusively looking at the carbon sequestration potential of rubber trees our study suggests if at all, to consider rubber trees an option in order to replace fruit tree plantations."\n      ]\n    },\n    {\n      "heading": "7.14 Land Use Change Impact Assessment model (LUCIA)",\n      "content": [\n        "To calibrate and parameterize the Land Use Change Impact Assessment model (LUCIA), knowledge about biomass and carbon stocks stored in vegetation is of importance.",\n        "As a first step our study generated basic primary data on behalf of biomass and carbon stocks of perennial land-use systems in the Chieng Khoi commune.",\n        "Furthermore our data helps to optimize and validate existing parameterization equations for different types of natural vegetation.",\n        "Secondly, our data can serve as baseline in order to estimate biomass and carbon stocks that exceed the typical rotation time of a respective land-use type.",\n        "Thirdly, our biomass and C-stock estimates of different fallow stages can be used as a first step in an approach to model and answer questions which concern the duration, climax and transition of different successive natural fallow phases, for example from grass fallow to bush fallow, or from pioneer forest to secondary and finally to primary forest.",\n        "Lastly, to give an outlook for LUCIA, a goal of further model development will be to develop functions that relate latter stages of natural fallow with different soil types and altitudinal belts."\n      ]\n    },\n    {\n      "heading": "7.15 Hypotheses posed",\n      "content": [\n        "Based on the results shown and the latter discussion, the hypotheses presented at beginning of this study could be answered as follows:",\n        "The assumed ranking of land-use types with regard to carbon densities can be approved as the estimated values in total MgC/ha were on average 0.97, 4.56, 6.37, 23.11, 59.35, 60.09, 193.65 and 274.09 for grassland, Chromolaena odorata (L.) fallow, Melastoma sanguineum Sims fallow, fruit tree plantations, timber tree plantations, grazed secondary forest, bamboo forest and protected natural forest, respectively.",\n        "With regard to hypothesis number two, i.e. inter-and intra-specific differences of 12 year old fruit- and timber tree plantations, timber trees stored significantly more carbon in AGB biomass than fruit trees. Hereby, timber trees sequestered in the given time period 2-3 times the amount of carbon compared to fruit tree systems. Furthermore timber trees can be categorized into fast (pine trees) and slow ( Chukrasia tabularis ) carbon accumulating land-use systems. Fruit trees did not show such distinct intra-specific differences in carbon stock accumulation. In this respect however, mango trees showed the highest carbon accumulation.",\n        "The third hypothesis assumed that when using time -averaged C-stock data, fruit- and timber tree plantations differ in carbon densities (Mg/ha) from extensively managed fallow land (forests) after a time period of 20 years. This assumption can only be partly affirmed. Significant differences were shown between fruit and timber trees and between fruit and extensively managed fallow lands (forests). Yet, no significant difference was found when comparing timber plantations and extensively managed fallow lands (forests). However, our results indicate, that land-use types such as teak and pine tree plantations have the potential to store as much or even more carbon in AGB than the sampled forest stands, over a time period of 20 years.",\n        "Our last hypothesis was that decreasing forest cover during the last decades in Chieng Khoi carbon stocks have not been decreased significantly due to the effect of reforestation by using tree plantations. This assumption has to be rejected as our results show that communal carbon stocks have declined from 1954 to 2007 by 61%, i.e. from 874 GgC to 343 GgC."\n      ]\n    },\n    {\n      "heading": "8 Conclusion",\n      "content": [\n        "Considering the economic potential of a global carbon trading system, monitoring and quantifying terrestrial carbon sinks, sources and stocks as a basis for a transparent, economic and beneficial crediting system will be vital. Agricultural lands are limited worldwide as they are in the commune of Chieng Khoi. However in the latter an important part comprises sloping land. Further unsustainable land use will increase the current problems of erosion and thus soil fertility depletion, resulting in the need of farmers to diversify their incomes. As shown CDM projects may be one option to provide new income opportunities through financial compensation and employment for the increasing population and at the same time help to use the land more sustainably.",\n        "Moreover, the detailed inventorying used in this study revealed important information in terms of variability of C stocks with perennial land use systems in the commune of Chieng Khoi. Allometric models developed in this study make a small contribution in reducing the lack of site- and species-specific allometric models. However, this type of inventories are impractical at regional scale. But the information collected here may contribute to further develop and calibrate broader scale methods based on remote sensing and modelling approches, such as LUCIA. \'Results generated from LUCIA can be applied for land use planning authorities in Yen Chau people committee, and may thus improve the management of natural resources for sustainable development in the Mountainous Region in Viet Nam\' (Nguyen 2009). Further studies aiming at analyzing the feasibility of C sequestration in land-use systems should focus on the long-term continuity of C storage and biodiversity, on the potential for belowground C sequestration, and on social factors that may influence adoption of C sequestration practices.",\n        "While finishing the writing of this study the 15 th COP is held in Copenhagen. World leaders are under their obligation to craft a treaty for a post-Kyoto era. In this context this study wants to close with an excerpt from UN Secretary-General Ban Ki-moon\'s speech held at the General Assembly 22 September 2009, \'Excellencies, […] If there is one lesson to be learned from the climate crisis and the other crises of the past year - food, fuel, flu, financial - it is this: We share one planet, one home. As people, as nations, as a species, we are in this together. Instead of demanding concessions from others, let us ask how we can contribute to the greater good.\'"\n      ]\n    },\n    {\n      "heading": "9 References",\n      "content": [\n        "ABDULLAH, L. and PUSPITASARI, D. 2007. Many mango tree plantations are scattered all over the commune. The observed plantations ranged from 5-69 years of age and the average area never exceeded 0.5 ha. Regarding management, most of the trees are pruned during their juvenile phase to achieve the wanted growth habit. Later the trees are pruned irregularly, only when necessary, e.g. removing a dead or old branches. When existing, the mid-storey is cut twice a year, depending on its vigor, but mainly shortly before harvest, to ease fruit harvest. Under-storey is grazed by cattle. Another observed form of mango production was the intercropping of young mango trees with maize or cassava, mainly on sloping fields.",\n        "The mango tree plantation selected for sampling was 69 years of age and comprised 40 trees. Slope was 0-9°. Trees had the largest DBH of all measured trees in this study, averaging 51.93cm (± 11.33cm). Average stand height was 13.1m (± 1.61m). The intertwined canopies formed a partially dense stand canopy with canopy openings only where trees had been removed. Due to the latter, mid- storey vegetation (height between 0.75-1.3m). which mainly included C. odorata , tree saplings and Amorphophallus konjac of the family Araceae showed a rather insular pattern of occurrence. As the plantation was also used for shading and grazing of cows and buffalo during the high sunshine ours of midday, the herbaceous under-storey vegetation was scarce. In addition, observations of bare soil or soil just covered by a layer of coarse litter, i.e. mainly fallen mango tree leaves, might be interlinked to the latter additional use of the mango plantation.",\n        "<!-- image -->",\n        "(a)",\n        "<!-- image -->",\n        "<!-- image -->",\n        "(b)",\n        "<!-- image -->",\n        "(c)",\n        "(d)",\n        "Figure 38 : Tree and subplot allocation in main plots 1 and 2 in mango plantation (69 years).",\n        "<!-- image -->",\n        "Figure 37 : Over- ( a ), mid- ( b ), under-storey ( c ) and coarse litter ( d ) found in 69 year old mango plantation.",\n        "<!-- image -->"\n      ]\n    },\n    {\n      "heading": "Plantations of Dimocarpus longan",\n      "content": [\n        "Longan trees were introduced to the local farmers in the early 1990s. Planting of most observed plantations still date back to this time. The oldest monitored plantation was 14 years old and the youngest 5 years of age. However, due to low yields farmers have not continued planting additional trees since and therefore most plantations date between 10-12 years of age. All observed plantations were kept free of mid-storey, thus only grass was growing between the trees. Like mango, longan tree plantations are grazed by cattle. Pruning of trees is done once or twice a year. The area of the observed plantations was on average less than 0.3 ha.",\n        "The longan plantation selected for setting up sample plots was located next to a farm house, surrounded by pasture land, a maize field, a teak plantation and by a secondary forest stand. Most trees measured showed a multi-stemmed habitus, often beginning to branch at base or shortly thereafter. The 2-6 stemmed trees quickly proceeded into the crown structure, with an average height, height till canopy and ground cover per tree of 1.61m(± 0.25m), 3.78m (± 0.51m) and 13.45m², respectively. Stand density was 600 trees/ha. According to farmers, fruit yield was low, as the trees suffered from drought during flowering. Moreover, early leaf drop and insect pests attacking the leaves were mentioned by the interviewed farmers as consequences and reasons, respectively. Mid-story vegetation was not present as farmers cut weeds such as Chromolaena odorata (L.) regularly for green mulch. Under-story vegetation mainly comprised grass species, which were regularly grazed. Coarse litter mainly included twigs and leaves of longan trees, but also dried debris of the cut Chromolaena odorata (L.) plants.",\n        "<!-- image -->",\n        "(a)",\n        "<!-- image -->",\n        "(b)",\n        "Figure 39: Over- ( a ), under-storey ( stem branching pattern of longan trees found in Chieng Khoi commune. b ) and coarse litter ( c ) found in 12 year old longan plantation, ( d ) Typical",\n        "<!-- image -->",\n        "<!-- image -->",\n        "<!-- image -->",\n        "Figure 40 : Tree and subplot allocation in main plots 1 and 2 in longan plantation (12 years).",\n        "<!-- image -->"\n      ]\n    },\n    {\n      "heading": "Mixed fruit tree plantations",\n      "content": [\n        "The number of mixed fruit tree plantations found in Chieng Khoi is rather small compared to longan and mango tree plantations. Mostly the plantation comprises a mix of mango, jackfruit, longan, and a few tamarind and guave trees. Mid-storey does hardly exist and grass is found as under-storey vegetation, which is grazed by cattle. Pruning of trees is done according to interviewed local farmers all year round, whenever necessary.",\n        "The area of the mixed fruit tree plantation (stand age 12 years), including the species Artocarpus ssp. , Magnifera indica , and Dimocarpus longan , was 0.26ha. Stand density was 475 trees/ha. Mid-storey was not present. Under-storey comprised mainly grasses, which were grazed by cattle. Coarse litter was mainly composed of leaves from the trees growing next to the sample subplots and from leaves of bamboo plants growing on the borders of the plantation. Height of the different tree species was on average, 5.83m (±0.87m), 4.25m (±1.01m) and 5.32m (±0.58m) for Artocarpus ssp ., Dimocarpus longan and Magnifera indica , respectively. DBH was 14.12cm (±2.59cm), 13.19cm (±2.12cm) and 11.92cm (±3.20cm) for Artocarpus ssp ., Magnifera indica and Dimocarpus longan , respectively.",\n        "<!-- image -->",\n        "a.",\n        "<!-- image -->",\n        "<!-- image -->",\n        "<!-- image -->",\n        "b.",\n        "c.",\n        "Figure 42 :Tree and subplot allocation in main plots 1 and 2 in mixed fruit tree plantation (10-12 years).",\n        "<!-- image -->",\n        "Figure 41 : Over- ( a ), under-storey ( b ) and coarse litter ( c ) found in 10-12 year mixed fruit tree plantation.",\n        "<!-- image -->"\n      ]\n    },\n    {\n      "heading": "Plantations of Musa ssp.",\n      "content": [\n        "Banana plantations are found widespread all over the commune. Often the plants are monocropped, but intercropping banana with pineapple or maize was also observed. Most farmers call at least one banana plantation their own. The area covered by single plantations was comparably small ranging from 0.02 to 0.1ha. Often the plantations were established on moderate to steep slopes. Banana are planted for fruit and feed production. Whereat fruits are sold on the local markets or kept for home-consumption, fresh banana pseudo-stem and leaves are feed to animals such as cattle and buffalo. In addition farmers remove old, decaying pseudo-stem after fruit ripening from the plantation and burn or use them as pig feed. The mid-and under-storey vegetation was mostly scarce or non-existing. This may be due to the reduced light conditions underneath the often densely planted stands and due to farmers removing all under-or mid-storey 2-3 times per year as part of their management.",\n        "Surrounded by maize and cassava plantations, the banana plant plots were set up inside of the banana plantation, which spanned an area of 0.059ha. Stand density was 2100 stools/ha. Banana plant height, number of suckers, and ground cover per plant was on average, 3.47m (± 0.71m), 2 (± 1) and 5.73m² (± 2.17m²). Three trees of the species Tamarindus indica , Magnifera indica and Citrus x grandis were growing within the banana plantation area. The banana plants were intercropped with Ananas comosus plants which grew in rows along the slope of 25°. The understory was very sparse and consisted only of a few creeping plants such as Lygodium scandens (L.) Sw. and grasses. The bare soil was loamy and had a beige brown color, unlike the red colored soil found next to the plantation. Coarse litter was little and comprised of leaves of the mango tree and banana plant debris, i.e. mainly leaves and peels of the barking pseudo-stems.",\n        "<!-- image -->",\n        "(a)",\n        "<!-- image -->",\n        "<!-- image -->",\n        "(b)",\n        "<!-- image -->",\n        "(c)",\n        "(d)",\n        "Figure 43 : ( a ) View from opposite side on to 2 year banana plantation. However, according to farmers their vigorous growth is outstanding compared to teak and Chukrasia tabularis (information of interviewed farmer). Pruning of the trees is done on a regular basis twice a year. The cut branches are left on the forest floor for drying before they are collected for fuel-wood. According to farmers, the low wood density of the trees has two negative effects, i.e. often bark and sap-wood are attacked by termites and ants and secondly in dry summers trees are vulnerable to wildfire. Mid-storey vegetation is cleared twice a year, mainly shortly before the collection of fuel-wood from tree pruning. Under-storey vegetation mainly comprises grasses and muscoid plant species.",\n        "The 12 year old pine plantation encompassed an area of 0.31ha. Elevation was 466m.a.s.l.. Stand density was 1000 trees/ha. The average height, DBH and ground cover per tree was 10.4m (± 0.2m), 21.0cm (± 4.9cm) and 14.19m², respectively. The intertwined canopies of the individual trees formed a dense overall stand canopy. Pine trees and mid-storey vegetation are pruned every half year. Whereat the cut branches are left on the forest floor for drying and subsequent collection as fuel wood, the other pruning leftovers are left as green mulch within the plantation. Mid- storey mainly included Chromolaena odorata (L.) , small shrubs, tree saplings, and cassava plants which were previously grown on the same area. Under-storey was mainly grass of the species Oplismenus compositus . Coarse litter mainly comprised pine needles.",\n        "<!-- image -->",\n        "(a)",\n        "<!-- image -->",\n        "(b)",\n        "<!-- image -->",\n        "<!-- image -->",\n        "(b)",\n        "Figure 46 : Tree and subplot allocation in main plots 1 and 2 of pine plantation (12 years).",\n        "<!-- image -->",\n        "Figure 45: Over- ( a ), mid- ( b ), under-storey ( c ) and coarse litter ( d ) found in 12 year old pine plantation.",\n        "<!-- image -->"\n      ]\n    },\n    {\n      "heading": "Plantations of Tectona grandis",\n      "content": [\n        "Teak plantations are the most observed land-use types regarding timber production in Chieng Khoi commune. However, the average area of individual plantations can be as small as 0.02 ha and doesn\'t exceed 1ha. According to farmers, most new plantations were planted from mid to late 1990s as part of the government program 327. This program fostered reforestation to control erosion and farmers responded to the incentive of receiving free tree seedlings. Nevertheless the selected fields for planting were often those with the least soil fertility, \'where even cassava wouldn\'t want to grow anymore\' (quote of interviewed farmer). The often observed heterogeneous and reduced tree development within the different plantations may be a result thereof. Teak trees themselves are often not pruned. Mid-storey vegetation is scarce and under-storey vegetation mostly lacking. Litter is also very little. However this may be due to the removal of leaves by farmers after the main leaf fall for subsequent burning. Teak trees are harvested selectively for the first time after 10-12 twelve years depending on the need and state of tree performance.",\n        "The sampled plantation was establishment 1998-1999. Teak trees themselves were not pruned. Thus the intertwined canopies formed dense stand canopy which excluded much sunlight from the plantation floor. Nearly no growth of mid- or under-storey vegetation was found in the center of the plantation. Most Mid and understory was found at the edges of the plantation. When observed, the heterogeneous distributed mid-storey mainly included Chromolaena odorata (L.) and a few thorny shrubs. Under-storey comprised small creeping vegetation. Coarse litter mainly consisted of leaves, twigs and seed capsules from teak trees. Clearing of mid and under-storey growth was done at least twice a year. In particular the leaves of the teak trees are removed and burned, to in this case avoid retention of water flowing along the slope to the fish pond at the bottom of the plantation. M. Siddique \u0379 ͷ ͺ",\n        "The conversion of monoculture rubber ( Hevea brasiliensis ) plantations into rubber-based agroforestry systems has become a common trend in forestry management in the past few decades. RubberFlemingia macrophylla (a leguminous shrub) systems are popular in southwestern China\'s Xishuangbanna region. The biogeochemical cycles of soil carbon and nitrogen in forests are mainly affected by their fractions. This study investigated the effect of introducing Flemingia macrophylla to rubber plantations of different ages on soil carbon and nitrogen fractions. The experimental treatments included Rͷ (young rubber plantation), RFͷ (young rubberFlemingia macrophylla system), R\u0378 (mature rubber plantation) and RF\u0378 (mature rubberFlemingia macrophylla system). The results showed that the introduction of Flemingia macrophylla to rubber plantations of different ages significantly changed soil carbon and nitrogen fractions, improved soil labile organic carbon and nitrogen contents, and ameliorated soil environments. The average soil microbial biomass organic carbon, nitrogen and nitrate-nitrogen in the Ͷ-ͷͶ cm soil layer during the experimental period was \u0379;.Ϳ%, ͻͻ.ͻ%, and \u0378ͷͺ.ͽ% higher in RFͷ than Rͷ, respectively, and \u0378\u0378.ͷ%, \u0378\u0378.\u0378%, and ͼͻ\u0378.\u0378% higher in RF\u0378 than R\u0378, respectively. Therefore, Flemingia macrophylla can be used as an alternative interplanted tree species within rubber plantations in similar environments of southeastern Asia.",\n        "Monoculture rubber ( Hevea brasiliensis ) plantations have rapidly expanded in the last few decades in southeastern Asia 1,2 . Approximately 90% of global natural rubber production is derived from plantations in this region (http://www.rubberstudy.com), accounted for an estimated 84% of the total global rubber plantation area in 2012 1,3 . Rapid growth in the Chinese economy has increased demand for natural rubber. In response to this demand, the natural tropical forests of southwestern China\'s Xishuangbanna region were deforested and replaced with more than 470,000 ha of rubber plantations, which equates to more than 24% of the total land area of the region 4 . The expansion of these rubber plantations has led to water loss and soil erosion 5,6 , environmental degradation 3,7 , and threatened environmental biodiversity . At present, rubber-based agroforestry systems are consid8 ered the best way to resolve the environmental problems associated with rubber monoculture. In recent years, the local government of Xishuangbanna proposed the development of environmentally friendly rubber plantations to reduce the water and soil losses and increase environmental biodiversity 9,10 .",\n        "Legume plants could greatly enhance ecosystem services. Lucerne ( Medicago sativa ) and erect milkvetch ( Astragalus adsurgens ) have the potential to phytoextract rhenium from coal fly ash-amended alkaline soils 11 . Alfalfa ( Medicago sativa L.) can improve its phosphorus acquisition by increasing specific root length and exuding gcarboxylates into the rhizosphere in phosphorus-deficient environments 12 . A combination of legumes grass species can enhance soil C and N storage, productivity, and diversity in semi-arid grasslands 13 . Flemingia macrophylla is used in traditional medicine for various therapeutic uses and is widely planted in the Xishuangbanna area of China. Flemingia macrophylla is a perennial leguminous leafy shrub with strong biological nitrogen fixation and",\n        "ͷ CAS Key Laboratory of Tropical Plant Resources and Sustainable Use, Xishuangbanna Tropical Botanical Garden, Chinese Academy of Sciences, Menglun town, Mengla county, Yunnan Province, ͼͼͼ\u0379Ͷ\u0379, China. \u0378 University of Chinese Academy of Sciences, Beijing, ͷͶͶͶͺͿ, China. \u0379 Yunnan Institute for Food and Drug Control, Kunming, Yunnan Province, ͼͻͶͶͷͷ, China. ͺ The UWA Institute of Agriculture, The University of Western Australia, Crawley, WA, ͼͶͶͿ, Australia. As a result, rubberFlemingia macrophylla intercropped systems have become popular in the Xishuangbanna area to improve soil carbon and nitrogen storage.",\n        "Precise and accurate estimations of carbon and nitrogen levels in forest soil are important for understanding biogeochemical cycles 15-17 . Some studies have indicated that soil organic carbon (SOC) has been depleted in rubber plantations 18,19 . Rubber plantations have 15% lower annual surface soil CO fluxes than natural forests, 2 because they have lower soil respiration during the dry season 20 . The conversion of tropical rainforests into rubber plantations has increased N O emissions, which may potentially enhance local climate warming trends 2 21 . Rubber plantations have lower mean CH4 uptake rates than secondary and tropical forests 22 . These transformations and biogeochemical cycles of soil carbon and nitrogen are mainly affected by their fractions 23-27 that play essential roles in the turnover of nutrients in soil, including water-soluble organic carbon (WSOC), light fraction of organic C (LFOC), microbial biomass organic C (MBC), ammonium N (AN), nitrate N (NN), light fraction of organic N (LFON), and microbial biomass organic N (MBN). These fractions are often used to study the impacts of land management and ecological succession 26,28,29 . Rubber-based agroforestry systems have higher SOC and nitrogen levels and lower carbon and nitrogen losses than rubber plantations due to improved soil macroaggregates 30 . However, little is known about the effect of these systems on soil carbon and nitrogen fractions.",\n        "The objectives of this study were to examine: (1) soil carbon and nitrogen fractions, and (2) the relationships between soil carbon and nitrogen fractionsin rubber and rubberFlemingia macrophylla plantations of different ages."\n      ]\n    },\n    {\n      "heading": "Results",\n      "content": [\n        "Soil carbon fractions. However, SOC content in the 0-10 cm soil layers was significantly higher than the 10-30 cm soil layers in each plantation type. In the 0-10 cm soil layer, RF1 had consistently higher WSOC content than R1 from June 2015 to January 2017, which differed significantly in August 2016 and January 2017. The WSOC content between R2 and RF2 did not differ significantly for the duration of the experiment (Table 2). In the 10-30 cm soil layer, the introduction of Flemingia macrophylla to the rubber plantations had no significant effect on WSOC content.",\n        "The 0-10 cm soil layer had significantly higher LFOC content than the 10-30 cm soil layer in each plantation type. The TN content in the 0-10 or 10-30 cm soil layers did not differ between the plantation treatments (R1, R2, RF1, RF2) for the duration of the study (April 2014 to January 2017) (Table 5). However, the 0-10 cm soil layer had significantly higher TN contents than the 10-30 cm soil layer in each plantation type. Plantations with suitable native, broad-leaved species (for example, Alnus subcordata C. A. Mey.) along with planned forestation management could potentially rehabilitate the degraded natural forests of northern Iran 31 . Mo and Sha 32 reported that adding carbon-sink plants into rubber plantations increased the soil organic carbon storage. The presence of legumes in semi-arid grasslands increased soil C and N storage by increasing the above- and below-ground biomass, litter biomass, plant species richness, and diversity 13 . In 2014 in present study, biomass of Flemingia macrophylla accumulated in the young and mature rubber plantations at 23.02 and 0.55 t ha -1 , respectively 14 but had no significant effect on SOC or TN contents. There are four possible explanations for this response: (1) the rubberFlemingia macrophylla system had only been established for seven years, and it is unlikely that soil C and N storage would have changed significantly in such a short time; (2) the high-temperature and high-humidity environments in the rainy season would promote the decomposition of plant residues and nutrient leaching, which is not conducive to C and N storage; (3) the introduction of Flemingia macrophylla to the rubber plantations reduced plant species richness and diversity (Fig. 5) and the input of litter biomass of other species; (4) most rubber plantations in this area have been planted on sloping land 20 . In our study, the rubber and rubberFlemingia macrophylla plantations were planted on sloping land between 47 and 58%, and the nutrients of the litter decomposition and some un-decomposed litter of Flemingia macrophylla would have been lost due to runoff.",\n        "While the introduction of Flemingia macrophylla to the rubber plantations had no significant effect on the C and N contents in the soil, the fractions of C and N changed significantly. The introduction of Flemingia macrophylla in the young rubber plantations was propitious for improving the labile organic C (WSOC and LFOC) and LFON contentin the 0-10 cm soil layersdue to the input of large amounts of litter from Flemingia macrophylla each year (Fig. 5C). The light fraction of the soil mainly consisted of plant residues, small animals, and microorganisms in various stages of decomposition. The microbial biomass of soil controls organic matter sequestration and decomposition and is generally used as an ecological sensitivity indicator for changes in soil nutrients due to land use practices 38,39 . For example, high MBC/SOC and MBN/TN ratios indicate that the organic matter decomposed quickly 40 . In the current study, the introduction of Flemingia macrophylla to the rubber plantations increased MBC and MBN, along with the MBC/SOC and MBN/TN ratios in the 0-10 and 10-30 cm soil layers. These results agree with those of Wen et al . 41 , who suggested that the conversions of monocultures into mixed forests had a strong positive effect on soil microbial biomass, and increased the efficiency of microbes in soil carbon decomposition along the profile by improving the ratios of the MBC/SOC and MBN/TN. It was observed that large amounts of leaves from the Flemingia macrophylla were incorporated into the soil each year, which tended to decrease the LFOC/LFON ratio in the soil due to the low C/N ratio in the leaves of Flemingia macrophylla . Generally speaking, the C/N ratio of Flemingia macrophylla is approximately 15:1 14 . Chen 42 and Huang 43 reported that low C/N ratios in soil could increase microbial biomass, and accelerate the decomposition of soil organic matter. We also observed a significant negative correlation between the ratio of LFOC/LFON, and MBC and MBN ( P ≤ 0.01). These results suggest that the decreased ratios of LFOC/LFON in the rubberFlemingia macrophylla plantations enhanced microbial activity."\n      ]\n    },\n    {\n      "heading": "Conclusions",\n      "content": [\n        "While the introduction of Flemingia macrophylla to the rubber plantations did not significantly increase total soil carbon or nitrogen levels over a short period, it changed the carbon and nitrogen fractions, improved labile organic carbon and nitrogen contents, and ameliorated the soil environment. We recommend that local governments and farmers in southeastern Asia use Flemingia macrophylla as alternative interplanted tree species within rubber plantations."\n      ]\n    },\n    {\n      "heading": "Materials and Methods",\n      "content": [\n        "Description of the study site. This study was conducted in the Xishuangbanna region (21°33 N, 101°28 E; ′ ′ 880 to 900 m asl) of Yunnan Province in southwestern China. This region has a typical tropical monsoon climate, with an annual mean temperature of 21.8 °C. The area receives mean annual precipitation of ~1,500 mm, 80% of which occurs in the rainy season (May to October) 44 . Furthermore, Xishuangbanna contains the largest area of tropical rainforests in China. Its biodiversity is rich as it is part of the Indo-Burma world biodiversity hotspot 45 . The soil has been classified as laterite (Oxisol), which developed from arenaceous shale sediment 44,46 .",\n        "In 1991 and 2003, the tropical forests with slopes ranging from 47-58% were deforested. Sugarcane ( Saccharum officinarum L.) was then planted annually. Rubber trees were plantedon these sites in May 1994 and 2006 at a density of 450 rubber trees ha -1 , with 8 m spacing between adjacent rows. In accordance with the local practices for rubber trees less than three years of age, fertilizers were applied between the rubber trees at depths of 20 cm using spades at rates of 27.0 kg ha -1 N, 5.9 kg ha -1 P , and 11.2 kg ha -1 K, which were split into two applications per year (May and October). Once the rubber trees were more than three years of age, the fertilizer application rates changed to 54.0 kg ha -1 N, 11.8 kg ha -1 P , and 22.4 kg ha -1 K. The rubber plantation farmers generally sprayed sulfur powder at 30-60 kg ha -1 yr -1 to control powdery mildew on the rubber trees. Weeds in the plantations were cut using a sickle twice per year (April/May and November/December) and left on the ground.",\n        "In July 2010, Flemingia macrophylla was introduced into the differently aged rubber plantations (4 and 16 years of age) at a density of 10,830 plants ha -1 . From 2011 onwards, the Flemingia macrophylla in the different rubber plantations was cut using a sickle in December each year and left as ground cover. From 2012 onwards, given the strong biological nitrogen fixation of Flemingia macrophylla , no additional N was applied in the rubberFlemingia macrophylla plantations. The inputs of P , K, and S in the rubberFlemingia macrophylla plantations remained the same as those in the adjacent rubber plantations.",\n        "Experimental design, sampling, and measurements. In this study, three replicate sites were selected within each rubber and rubberFlemingia macrophylla plantation (Fig. 5). Each replication site consisted of 20 × 25 m 2 survey plots (four rows of rubber trees, and three 8-m wide hedgerows) containing nine sampling subplots (8 × 6 m 2 ), with three located at each slope position (upper, middle, and lower slope).",\n        "For each of the nine subplots, soil samples were collected using a soil auger, avoiding the fertilization holes, at two depths (0-10 cm and 10-30 cm) after carefully removing the litter-fall and/or grass layer. For each replicate site in the different rubber and rubberFlemingia macrophylla plantations, soil core samples were collected in April 2014, June 2015, January and August 2016, and January 2017. The nine soil cores were combined into a composite sample, which were air-dried, ground, and sieved (at < 2 mm) for analysis of ammonium nitrogen (AN) and nitrate-nitrogen (NN), and the light fraction organic carbon (LFOC) and nitrogen (LFON). The sieved samples were sieved again (at < 0.25 mm) for determination of soil organic carbon (SOC) and total nitrogen (TN). For soil water-soluble organic carbon (WSOC), microbial biomass carbon (MBC) and nitrogen (MBN) measurements, the samples were taken to the laboratory and stored at 4 °C for subsequent analyses.",\n        "The SOC and TN of the bulk soil were determined using a Vario MAX CN-Analyzer (Elementar Analysensysteme GmbH, Germany). A density fractionation scheme for light was used following the method described by Gregorich and Ellert 47 . During fractionation, 25 g of air-dried soil was shaken with 50 mL of NaI solution (sp.Gr. = 1.70) for 60 min. After centrifugation, the supernatant was passed through a Millipore filter (0.45 µ m) and the light fraction collected. The soil residue in the centrifuge was extracted again with NaI, and the additional light fraction collected. The light fraction was oven-dried at 60 °C of 72 h. The concentration of organic carbon and nitrogen was determined by dry combustion using a Vario MAX CN-Analyzer (Elementar Analysensysteme GmbH, Germany). Dried samples, each weighing 5 g, were added to 50 mL of 2 M KCl, shaken for one hour, and analyzed with an Auto Analyzer 3 (SEAL Analytical GmbH, Germany) to determine AN and NN contents 34,48 . Microbial biomass carbon (MBC) and nitrogen (MBN) in the soil were estimated using a fumigation-extractionmethod 49 that included a purified CHCl 3 treatment, followed by a 0.5 M K SO extraction of fumigated and unfumigated soil 2 4 50 . After which, soil samples (equivalent to 25 g of dry soil weight) were fumigated for 24 h at 25 °C with CHCl (ethanol-free). Following fumi3 gant removal, the soil was extracted with 100 mL of 0.5 M K SO by shaking for 1 h at 200 rpm, followed by filtering. 2 4 The non-fumigated portions were extractedat similar time intervals. Following the extraction, MBC and MBN contents were measured by determining the C and N masses in the filtrate using a Vario TOC cube-Analyzer (Elementar Analysensysteme GmbH, Germany). Microbial biomass carbon was calculated as follows: MBC = (Corg(fum) -Corg(non))/0.38 51 . Microbial biomass nitrogen wascalculated as follows: MBN = (TN(fum) -TN(non))/0.45 51 . The ratios of MBC to total carbon (MBC/SOC) and MBN to total nitrogen (MBN/TN) were then calculated. Water-soluble organic carbon (WSOC) was extracted from field-moist samples within 24 h of sampling by shaking 15 g soil with 30 mL distilled water for 2 h at 25 °C, followed by centrifugation at 5000 r min -1 at 4 °C for 15 min. The supernatant was filtered through a 0.45 µ m carbon-free membrane. The filtrates were stored at 4 °C and analyzed within 24 h using a Vario TOC cube-Analyzer (Elementar Analysensysteme GmbH, Germany) 35 .",\n        "Statistical analysis. The data were subjected to analysis of variance (ANOVA) using SAS statistical analysis software version 8.0. One-factor ANOVA was deployed to compare treatment effects. The least significant difference (LSD at 0.05 level of probability) test was applied to assess the differences between means. Pearson\'s coefficient analysis was used for correlation."\n      ]\n    },\n    {\n      "heading": "References",\n      "content": [\n        "- 1. Warren-Thomas, E., Dolman, P. M. & Edwards, D. P.
